This is one of the few games my wife plays. Why does she play it? Because she loves politics and this title is just oozing theme. Every four years around elections time, this games hits the table with weekly regularity. 

Making of the President is also very educational, as it serves as a reminder how the electorial college operates.

I find the game gets better the more you play it. Learning the cards adds to the strategy.

RATING: 7.75
I think this a fun game, and I love how the mechanics represent the theme so well.  I love the narrative and tactical choices inherent in the cards.  The game has all the highs and lows (and luck) which I think must be present on the campaign trail, and because of that I find myself exhausted by the end of the game. 

At 4 plays so far, it is hard for me to determine good strategies to use amidst all the chaos and luck the cards introduce.  Sometimes I feel I have absolutely no control at all, but again I feel this really fits with the game's theme.

My husband and I are both enjoying this, but I can't rate it higher yet because we need to have a lot of energy, and the right mood, to bring this to the table.  



=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
First off, I like Matthews' style of area control CDGs. I like the theme and I think the cards and actions largely reflect that. There are some things that I find unfortunate about the game. The debate phase is sort of a nuisance here as we almost always end up with 1 or 2 cards that are just sort of nuked, but using it as a "Space race/Tiananmen sq" type safety valve really spices it up as you aren't quite sure how it's going to play out. The bummer is that this requires both players have a similar mind set. Second, is there isn't really a reshuffle ala TS's Early/Mid/Late war system. If I get the right cards, I'm in like Flynn, but if I don't, there is only so much I can do about it. It's not like (as Nixon) I have to worry about Hostile Press Corps coming back to haunt me in a couple turns if I play it for points now and pre-empt it. Last, and particularly unfortunate given the second point is how the events fire off. It's bitter sweet in that it seems rather easy to maneuver around someone else's events so I don't have to worry about dealing with the consequences of them (especially if I chuck them on the debates), but this becomes much more of an issue before election day as you're more likely to pick cards for states more so than to dodge them and it's a more critical time for plays with time running out. If you're out of momentum markers, then it's open season for me, where as in TS I still have to deal with it but get to choose whether I get the points first or you get the event. That's a nuance to the general system that I find missing. On the other hand, I do like the system surrounding the momentum markers and the issue track, that's an interesting form of brinksmanship in terms of what sort of bait can you do to get your opponent to use their markers so you can sneak stuff in. One other thing in particular that I like is I find it's really easy and plays fairly fast (compared to his other games). The hovering around 0 and flipping things with endorsements is also a nice touch and makes election day worthwhile, but that sort of requires people play toward that strategy though...

I think there are two times when I'd play 1960 over Twilight Struggle; if I'm teaching someone new to Matthews' game style, and/or if I'm short on time. I find 1960 can be setup, played, and finished in 2 hours compared to the 3-5 for the other two games. Second, it's much easier to explain this as the influence placement and event firing are much more simplistic. It's certainly enjoyable, but all things equal and possible, it's probably not the first Matthews game I'd reach for.

update: 15+ plays in, I think it's a game where the meta-strategy and subtle nuance of CP usage in states is really a factor, more so than in either of it's bigger siblings. Will my opponent also chuck my stuff into the debates? Will I try and lock up stuff and dislodge them near the end? Will I compete on issues or do without much in the way of momentum to instead spend those cards in states? When you pick different strategies, the game sort of unravels a bit, but if people play in a similar fashion, it seems to really be a great nail biter in a smaller package. I almost want to talk about what I'm going to do before starting...  Still a 7, but maybe a 6 or an 8 at times depending who I'm playing against and how close it is going into election day (it's an 8 if I can ultimately win or lose based on how election day falls out by flipping, or failing to flip, a state through support checks).

update2: The GMT tweaks are welcomed, but ultimately the delicate meta of "split the Northeast and use card effects to efficiently snatch states elsewhere" only goes so far in my personal interest level. Docked to a solid 6 (and finally sold my copy to a friend whom I may borrow it from occasionally).

RATING: 9.8
[So close to my second 10.] The clearest rulebook I’ve read in a game, component-wise errata-free and heavily nuanced, this GMT Games version of 1960 is not only highly playable—it’s a snap to get underway and collectively, that’s why it almost earns a full mark. Fine out of the box, numerous avenues towards victory. If I were to improve it by way of a future “Deluxe +” version, I’d dump the State Seal tokens, mount the Debate Board and replace the Momentum counters with bespoke quality Poker chips. Easily playable solitaire, this game of course shines with two. This is a great remake and its subtle rules tightening works exceedingly well. Some bona fide solitaire rules would seal this second term.

It remains my medium weight 2-player game of choice (of which I could use a few more). And again the theme is really great.

RATING: 8.48
Rating will likely change after repeated plays, but I get the sense that this will be a high 7s to mid 8s kind of game.  

It’s hard to not immediately try to compare this game with [i]Twilight Struggle[/i], at least mechanically, but there are key differences which makes this one a little less tense and agonizing in the decision space, and a little less susceptible to longer-term strategizing.  For example, players use about 88 cards in a game from a 97-card deck.  There are a couple events that allow you to discard cards and redraw replacements, but it is quite likely that you will never reshuffle the discard pile.  This means getting rid of an opponent’s card to the discard pile is much more safe than in [i]Twilight Struggle[/i] (there are a couple more events that allow a card to be pulled from discard, so not completely safe). The deck is also not split into an “Early Campaign,” “Mid-Campaign,” and “Late Campaign” decks, meaning you really can’t plan for certain events to appear at certain times in the game, nor are you guaranteed to see any particular event in any particular game.  This means sometimes debate events are drawn after the debates, and prevention events are drawn after all the events they are intended to prevent have already occurred. Additionally, the momentum mechanic limits your ability to trigger your events played by your opponent (but also allows you to preempt certain of your opponent’s events from occurring when you play them).  This mechanic actually results in some interesting decisions, as you may try to play a somewhat favorable event for your opponent early in a turn to tempt them to spend their momentum, just so you can dump devastating events later when they have no momentum to trigger them. 

This is much more approachable than [i]Twilight Struggle[/i], both in depth and the fact that the theme is less off-putting (to those that find the cold war theme off-putting).  Need to play more to assess depth.  Also might be nice to have a way to track a running tally of projected electoral votes as the game progresses.

RATING: 7
1st play: I spent the first part of the game constantly comparing to ways it didn't line up with Twilight Struggle.  By the time we were finished, I really appreciated the game for it's own sake.  It doesn't have quite the narrative arc that TS does, but it plays in about half of the time, and the simplifications don't really remove the strategy.  I really like the idea of support checks instead of dice rolls, although the dice roll makes for more exciting moments.  Similar to Twilight Struggle, after playing this game I'm eager to play again, and I like reading about the cards to learn about events from this era of our history.  Top things learned: California was Nixon's home state, the south is predominantly Democrat, and Vermont and New Hampshire were Republican.  Something about Oregon and Washington being Red states took some getting used to also!

2nd play: Played again on the last day of the year! Excellent game and we corrected a few minor rules mistakes from the first play.  Not quite as good as Twilight Struggle, but still really good.  The theme is timely and gives a real sense for just how close presidential races can be.  The cardplay makes more sense when we properly prepare for debates, and the end game checks were tense.  Wish this one weren't so expensive to pick up, it's worth owning alongside Twilight Struggle.

RATING: 8
[b]After 1 play:[/b]
It's a great game. Our second CDG, after Hannibal: Rome vs Carthage. I cannot speak of 1960, without referring to H:RvC.
Similitudes: both are CDG, you battle for influence in areas, somewhat different starting positions (especially Hannibal).
Differences: theme (d'oh!), the fact that the opponent can trigger an event from a card that you played as CP (excellent mechanism!), much simpler and clearer rules, short duration (our first game lasted only 2 hours!), not too many strategies to choose from.

RATING: 7
Things I like:  Relatively short play time (vs others of the genre), interesting cards & historical aspect, angst of what to do with your opponents cards that you have drawn.
Things I dont like:  Potential for issues dominance & back and forth nature of the issues.  Rest cubes dont seem to do nearly enough to justify it.  Game seems to be largely determined by who draws more powerful cards, and who draws their opponents power cards in the final two turns when they can be buried without consequence.

RATING: 7
Excellent game with exceptionally smooth game play, fantastic mechanics, and well-developed thematic elements. The theme is presented in actual news articles, which drive event abilities - so if you want, you can learn about what happened in 1960 via flavor text. The rule book is better than average, although it could be better organized. The only reason for a slightly lower rating on my part is that details of American elections are a passing curiosity for me at best, so it doesn't hold my interest from a thematic perspective.

RATING: 7
At the time of writing, Twilight Struggle is one of three games I rate a 10.  1960 was almost bound to suffer by comparison.  The single campaign deck that lasts most of the game without a reshuffle takes away the deck management of Twilight Struggle.  Decision making seems a lot more straightforward too.  Nixon is strong in the West, Kennedy in the South, so a lot of the contest is fought over a few key states in the East and the Mid-West.  Luck of the draw can have a powerful influence in Twilight Struggle and in 1960 it feels even stronger.  

Thematically the game is very nice, with period photos, snippets of flavour text and good artwork. The rest/momentum system is a very clever innovation.  As others have commented, the absence of a running score leaves players to either wing it and hope for the best or spend ages counting.  More clearly visible state values would have been good too.   1960 is merely good where Twilight Struggle was great.  It's main advantage over its older cousin is a much shorter playing time.

RATING: 7
A very good 2p game. Ultimately the game comes down to clever hand management of the cards, and deciding how to use the cards etc. for your best advantage. In that way, the political theme matches the gameplay. I don't like the way that mechanisms that feel like they should be more important (e.g. the debates) aren't, it's counter-intuitive, and it's also very hard, without more experience, to know what the best strategies and moves might be, so it's all a bit of a shot in the dark until the final count. ...

Probably debates are more important than I thought. However, as I now prefer Twilight Struggle, this won't get much play, except when there isn't time for TS or someone else wants to play this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I first played 2008: Campaign Manager and then Founding Fathers, so was very much looking forward to this one.

The game is considerably heavier than either game mentioned, but this is mostly due to having to keep track of such a bigger board and in the many more options you are presented with each turn.  The base mechanics are very simple to understand however (card driven, play a card for it's event or for it's CP) and all of the options interact with each other very well; allowing players to cleverly manipulate various aspects of the board state (mostly in how players gain control of each state).

Overall I really like this game.  But there are a few issues it suffers from.  One comes in terms of components in that there really needs to be a bit more cubes (perhaps 10 more for each player) to help prevent shortages.  Another comes from how certain aspects feel a bit too lucky, this can come from card draw (where it can really hurt you to not have enough cards to both save for the debates/ election and in draws where all of your events only seem to benefit your opponent) and in various 'checks' where random cube draws dictate whether actions are successful and who goes first.

Despite these issues however, 1960 excels at so many levels.  It is both very flavorful and well integrated in its theme, it's components are all very functional with one another (if the cubes are a bit lacking), and it's mechanics are easy to follow and allow for a number of different avenues for victory.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
I've had this and twilight struggle for a while, but hadn't been able to get them to the table - they are two player games that take a significant time sink to play. Well, I finally had a game day where people had to bail until it was only me and one other player, and I decided to bring this one. Wow, this game is tense and full of theme. I was Nixon, he was Kennedy and we vied for control of the east and the midwest and it just felt like a true battle. The choices were meaningful (do I play this for ops...uh oh Kennedy benefits big time on this *gulp*). I mean, I don't think there was a turn where either of us were on autopilot. I went into the debates feeling like this was going to be a close contest, then was CLEAN SWEPT on all three issues, and suddenly Kennedy had everything but the west locked up. I can't wait to either get this to the table again (Nixon shall riiiiiiiise again!), or get Twilight Struggle to the table. Holding off on an official rating though because I've only played once, but tentatively I give this a 9.

RATING: 8.5
The best election game that I know.

It plays fast, it's well balanced, and easy to explain and play.

Unlike Twilight Struggle, where the history aspect really creates an atmosphere, in here it doesn't help much for it is too much of a specific moment in history, American history. So, it's kind of hard for me and my brazilian gaming group to relate to the facts and people.
But the use of midia, relevant issues, endorsements, debates, etc. really create the atmosphere of an electoral duel.

It's also a great game to introduce hard core eurogamers to card-driven "wargames". They start with 1960, then migrate up to Twilight Struggle, and, when they least expect, they are happily playing Paths of Glory and Hannibal...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Vaya por delante que el tema, para mí, es una NO rotundo. Sin  embargo siempre está bien escuchar a los demás... y las críticas eran TAN buenas!!!
El juego es fenomenal. Recrea la campaña por la presidencia entre Nixon y Kennedy.

La partida está dividida en 4 espacios temporales: la campaña electoral, un debate televisivo, el sprint final de la campaña y el resultado electoral. 
Los turnos constan de tres fases (a grandes rasgos): robar cartas, jugarlas y una última fase donde dependiendo de la política del candidato se reparten beneficios en forma de apoyos electorales o de impulsos (que no dejan de ser esfuerzos del candidato por ocultar sus debilidades o hacerse con políticas jugadas por el oponente). 

Las cartas se gastan de dos maneras:
- como puntos para ser gastados en mítines (que consiguen hacer retroceder el apoyo electoral al rival o hacer avanzar al tuyo en el mapa de EEUU) o también para enfatizar el tipo de política (derechos civiles, defensa o economía). 
- O como el texto que trae cada carta, que será pro Kennedy, pro Nixon, o a favor de los dos. Todos los textos son sucesos que ocurrieron durante la campaña y siempre salen todas las cartas en cada partida.

Despues del turno 5, llega el Gran Debate televisivo, una fase que tiene su enjundia y que aunque no me parece excelentemente bien resuelta (al menos por ahora) le da una pausa y un aroma a la partida que me chifla.

Le siguen dos turnos más, que son el sprint de campaña, con alguna regla especial chiquitilla, y por último el resultado de las generales, con un recuento de votos de mucha tensión, donde el número que se perigue es ¿267?. (creo que sí).
Pedazo de juego para dos, terriblemente inmersivo, de reglas fáciles, difícil de controlar pero que jugado con calma es estupendo. Duración cortuca-media y con un tempo y una sensación de baile de salón, donde todo fluye, donde todo está relacionado y la lógica lo domina todo... Un aroma excelente... (Salvo cuando en los dos últimos turnos, una vez acabado el debate televisivo, se roba alguna carta que apoya ese debate, y que a mí juicio, me saca completamente de esa fluidez)

Es un placer jugar al jueguito. Me recuerda muchísimo al Twilight Struggle aunque no sé por qué, éste de las elecciones me gusta más. Todo será jugar más al TS!

RATING: 10

RATING: 9
Szerintem zseniális a játék, bizonyos elemeiben még a Twilight Struggle-t is felülmúlja. Azt hittem, sokkal jobban hasonlít egymásra a két játék, ehhez képest csak néhány főbb mechanizmus a közös. Nemcsak a téma alapvetően más, hanem a lehetséges akciók is nagyon eltérőek. Ami szerintem jobb, mint a TS-ben, hogy itt az események kiváltása az ellenfél részéről nem automatikus, hanem fizetni kell érte. Tetszik az is, hogy fix körös a játék, a történelemtől ez esetben legfeljebb a végeredményben térünk el, bár a TS-ben meg épp az tetszik, hogy sok féle vége lehet. Mindkettőnek megvannak a maga előnyei. Jó ötlet még a zsák, és a bábu mozgatása is. Lehet vitatkozni a szerencse mértékén, szerintem a zsákos megoldás jobb, mint a kocka. A 6. forduló-beli vita szerepe kicsit túlértékeltnek tűnik így elsőre. A játék 2/3-ában főként a vitára készülünk, ehhez képest a díjazás annyi, amennyit akármelyik kártyával is megkaphatunk. Sokkal fontosabb ennél, hogy melyik 4 államban javíthatunk a választás napján. A lendület jelzők szerepe helyenként fontos, de sokszor nem tudtam őket mire használni, mert nem volt értelmes esemény. Egy megjegyzés az esélyekről: elsőre Nixon kicsit erősebbnek tűnik, ami azért meglepő, mert a valóságban Kennedy nyert. A TS-ben jobban el vannak találva a valóságos viszonyok, hiszen az Usa ott a vége felé kezdi kapni a jobb kártyákat, az 50-es években még a szovjetek az erősebbek.

Ha már végig a TS-hez hasonlítottam, akkor az összegzésnél is így teszek: az 1960 jóval könnyedebb, kisebb lélegzetvételű, számomra átláthatóbb, kiszámíthatóan 2-3 órás játékidővel (szemben a TS kb. 1-4 órájával), és szerintem főleg a témán múlik, hogy ki melyikkel ül le játszani. Nem gondoltam, hogy a TS után még ennyi újat fogok látni egy hasonló gyökerű játéktól.

Ja, a kártyák (a többi kártyavezérelt játékhoz hasonlóan) nagyon jól néznek ki. És sajnos nyelvfüggőek.
---
Továbbra is zseniális...
---
Egy fontos adalék még, szintén a TS-hez viszonyítva: míg a TS-ben egy kártya nagy eséllyel többször is játékba kerülhet, de nagy esély van arra is, hogy fele nem kerül játékba (ha előbb vége van), itt elég pontosan ki van számolva, hogy pont akkor fogy el a pakli, amikor vége a játéknak +- 1-2 lap. Tehát kb. 99%-os valószínűséggel egy lap pontosan egyszer kerül játékba.

RATING: 10
A játék fogadtatása részemről elég vegyes volt. Nem voltam oda a témáért, de ez egy játékot követően teljesen megváltozott. Azóta sok mindennek utána néztem - már ezért megérte kipróbálnom...
Számomra nagyon sok újdonságot tartalmaz mechanizmusaiban.
Tetszik, hogy vmi azonnali jóról mindig le kell mondani egy jövőbeni remélt még jobb érdekében. 
Sok mindent szem előtt kell tartani, és figyelni kell a másik játékos terveire is. Ötletes a vitaesemény, és a választás napja is.
Egyelőre nem is ismerek más olyan játékot, amely az első lap kijátszásától olyan feszültség alatt képes tartani, mint ez...
A játék közben néha sírni tudnék, néha az öröm vetne szét - iszonyat érzelmeket képes a felszínre hozni!
Azt hiszem ezt is magammal vinném egy lakatlan szigetre, hogy legyen mivel játszani Péntekkel.

Azonban a szerencse mértéke - mely képes a szavazatszámokat jelentősen megváltoztatni - jelentősnek mondható,... de, hát ez mégis csak egy kártyajáték és ilyen maga az élet! Egyszer lent, egyszer fent...!
Nem volt még olyan partim, hogy ha a játék egyik fordulójában "elvett volna vmit", azt később "ne adta volna vissza"...

Fantasztikus!
Számomra vitathatatlanul a Nr.1!!! (2014-ben ez már csak a múlt... időközben a Netrunner "beelőzött")

RATING: 6.5
Well, this improves on a bunch of the aspects I dislike from Twilight Struggle. The static scoring allows for more long-term strategic board play as opposed to more tactical scoring. The limited number of opposing event activations means that you can plan your turn out a bit easier and also means that the game is less reliant on pure card memorization. Even though the advertised time of 90 minutes is correct, it might still play a bit too long for what it is. My wife, though, hated her plays of it (she's not played Twilight Struggle). If she didn't have such a distaste for it, I'd rate it a bit higher.

RATING: 5.5

The game mechanics make sense within the theme, but it didn't make for a very interesting game.

With the rise of the Soviet Union and civil rights in the American South threatening to reshape party ideologies, who would come out on top in this landmark contest? =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
1960 The Making of a President is one of those games that I would like always carefully select to buy and own. When I buy games I go by the following criteria

1) Gameplay feel
- First I always look at the game and feel would this be interesting, is winning this game a challenge, is it interesting and involving how would it feel to play this game and would there be any problems game play wise...

2) Replayability
- This one for me is huge.  Cause games for me could be good but maybe the replayability just aint there. Maybe you have that feel have seen it all and the path to "victory" or entire fee of the game would feel to samey.... 

3) Ease of Play
- Is the game clunky. Not just in the sense of is it a gateway game...no in general is it a chore going through the turn, is it too lengthy, is it too hard to explain it to people without them studying it themselves, are there any doesnt make sense parts in it and so on. If game flows great and makes sense.... Ease of play is there and that is a huge plus when introducing game to any new player.

4) Artistic feel
- Let's kid not...the game needs to have that looks great on the table aspect...well I mean it always helps. 

5) Thematic ties
- To really feel involved to play the game and feel immersed this is essential... must have almost for me.


This game?

Gameplay feel is definitely an 8. 
Replayability is probably a 7
Ease of play probably an 8,5
Artistic feel for me personally an 8 
Thematic tie is a 9,5

Which for me makes it  solid 8 score game. I was expecting an interesting game, studied it a lot before buying it, but it still "pleasantly surprised me" and I am urging to play it again soon it is the replayability which is probably the lowest because of the 2 player nature and just two possible roles, same starting positions and what not. But still many elements gets mixed and strategy is adapted and changes based on them a bit. so I do feel it has a fair amount of that as well. So like I said will gladly play it again. A great game and one I am glad to have in my collection

RATING: 8
Quite possibly the best game I've played in the last year.  There is nothing I can complain about with this game, except for the inherent frustration of a back-and-forth, knock down election struggle.  For every brilliant maneuver you make to surge ahead in the polls, your opponent will retaliate with an equally vicious play to knock you back down a notch.  Great, great fun.  Games easily coming in under 2 hours is a bonus.

After several more plays, my opinion of this is slightly less.  It's good, but it's not GREAT.  There are a few things that bother me - the debates seem really arbitrary, media influence seems all but useless, and positioning on the issues is very hit-and-miss.  It's also frustrating how easy it is for your opponent to just go around reversing all of your moves.  There are so many "add support" events that carrying a state has no real benefit.  Overall, still a good game but not quite as good as it could be.

RATING: 7
A recreation of perhaps the closest Presidential election in U.S. history, 1960' is an excellent introduction to the card-driven boardgames (Twilight Struggle) and wargames (Hannibal) that are becoming increasingly popular. Z-Man has given the game a lot of thematic flavor of the era with its graphics for the map and cards and the game itself can be finished in about 90 minutes between experienced players. Compared to other games with similar mechanics, this is a fraction of normal playing times by anywhere from a half to a quarter or even less. My first game clocked in at just two hours and came down to the Late Returns from Cook County and the Recount cards! (Kennedy won. I played Nixon.)

RATING: 9

Top-notch production, tricky Dick, savory heartache at what to do with some of those great events. . . this was a knockout for me. RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Both historical and playable, providing balanced play throughout with event card options seemingly evenly spread with influence and power for both players, reminding me in this respect of Hannibal: Rome versus Carthage. Also has the Hannibal feel to it with the card play centering around regions/states containing the highest electoral votes, (like the red/blue control counters in Hannibal). Rules are very clearly presented and seemingly easy to teach. I like how the debate turn provides a short break toward the end, and allows for a turn of events if the candidate played cards prudently the previous turns leading up to the debate. Good tactical play with the event cards is necessary, and should ultimately decide who will prevail in the final election turn. Neat mechanic includes the option to play on your opponent's card during his turn - and the ability to sometimes stop this if attempted.

RATING: 7.5
After having played it six times, I've generally found this game to be relatively engaging. The CDG mechanism is adapted nicely to the less war-like theme, and the "rest cube" bag mitigates the effects of bad card draws nicely.

My one critique is that it is simply too easy to travel all over the map and undo what your opponent has done. As a result, it has far more of a tit-for-tat feel than the gradual build of Twilight Struggle (which is, in my opinion, a better game).

RATING: N/A
Christmas 2007 - BGG Secret Santa
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Currently my favorite CDG. I grokked it fairly quick and played it online a lot before buying a physical copy. I like the theme, know the cards, and 96% of the rules fit on 1 page. Unfortunately, it's hard to bring to the table. It plays fast, I find I can play ten turns of this in two hours but 1989: Dawn of Freedom & Twilight Struggle take twice as long, barring any quick early game blowouts. Component-wise, the game is solid and the board is well designed.

Some negatives include theme and the Issues track. A common complaint is, the prevailing strategy of CP usage on the Issues track leads to repetition and boring play. I recognize that this can happen, I've gone issue heavy in many games myself, and the Issues are important but I don't think it breaks the game. Campaigning and using your cards well can balance out a lack of cubes in the Issues. Unfortunately, the theme is harder to overcome. If you don't find the most, well possibly the most, contentious American election in modern times interesting then this theme is not for you.

I'm a fan of the theme. All the cards work the election into their CP value and event. The art and photos on the cards are from the election as well. This is the 3rd game that Mr. Matthews designed that successfully melds theme and rules onto the cards.

RATING: 8.5
While you're inevitably reminded of Twilight Struggle (two player 'political' CDG covering a similar era) 1960 is sufficiently different in play that the superficial resemblance fades as you get into the game.  

I would say that it's a little less complex than TS (though perhaps someone playing this first then TS may feel the opposite) - it's certainly less footery (the cubes are much easier to handle than TS's chits, and there's not as much of them as the areas are not multi-occupancy) and the systems feel a bit more rounded, and possibly better integrated - the debate and final surge are nice touches, while the advertising/opinion battle offer a respectful nod to the grandaddy of political game Die Macher without being at all beholden to it.  At the moment I would say it's a slightly better game but I suspect that more plays of TS in particular could convince me otherwise - that element of unexplored depths is a major attraction of both games (though I suspect that TS is deeper as well as more complex - the two don't always go together).

Recommended - even if, like me, you've no idea where you were when JFK was shot (though in my cot is a good guess).

RATING: 5
UPDATE: I've taken my rating down ... 1960 just seems kind of, well, boring. Play some cubes, pick up some cubes, look for the good events ... there just is not that much excitement in the game. A lot of it I blame on the fact that advertising and issue support seem too weak, rarely worth investing in, and without those two dimensions the game is almost completely 1-dimensional and uninteresting. Maybe I'm jaded. It's OK, maybe systemically a little stronger than Twilight Struggle (although too many things are still out of whack), but TS has a more entertaining theme (although both themes are a bit of a paste-up). Not terrible, I might play it again, but not ultimately a keeper. Of the two of Z-Man's recent endeavors, Duel in the Dark is much more interesting.

Several usability foul-ups mar an otherwise attractive game. Also, there are definitely worries about play balance.
---
UPDATE: There are definitely bits of this game that make me uneasy. The cards do not seem well-balanced; the events are usually significantly more powerful than the CPs, to it's not usually much of decision whether to take the CPs or take the event, you just take the event if you qualify for it, which is a little weak. Advertising seems little weak. The Momentum chits are hit-and-miss, you seem to get more of them than you get opportunity to use them. The Support Check rules seem superflous since they don't apply to cubes placed as events. And it seems like a little bit too much of the strategy will involve knowing the deck, and what the event composition means in terms of the viability and importance of campaigning in various theatres (South, Northeast, etc).

I think it'll be a fun game for a while, probably moreso than Twilight Struggle just because of the length and because it's somewhat less one-dimensional. But I fear this will lack replayability for the same reasons TS did (large luck elements, opacity of the draw deck making the game needlessly difficult to learn for new players, and systemic imbalance).
---
Initial impressions are reasonably positive. Struggles with a few of the same issues Twilight Struggle had (some powerful neutral events are very swingy, even in such a straightforward game there are still elements that should have been removed), but the additional dimensions of endorsements, media, and issues seems to help quite a bit. Not totally sold yet, I definitely have some uneasiness about some significant things (play balance, balance between the game elements, luck level), but there is the possibility that this has managed to grasp what eluded Twilight Struggle. At the very least, the playtime and pacing are both much better.
---
Some graphic design issues with this game: while it's very attractive, what is the single most important piece of information in the game? How much a state is worth, point-wise. So why is that in tiny type? Come on guys, this is not rocket science.

RATING: 6
Updated 6/17/10.

Finally decided to sell this one after not playing it for something close to five months.  My general thoughts follow.

My wife and I went through several evolutions of strategy in learning 1960.  Early games focused on cube trading in large states.  Shortly thereafter, we discovered the strength of advertising and issue positioning, which changes the dynamic of the endgame a bit.  By the end, we had learned enough the cards to understand which of the smaller states were in play at which times, and then spent a good deal of energy on the issues to force or preclude those events.  

Although it was interesting to proceed through this development, the final stage is a bit of a rough one to stomach.  With more momentum markers in play, hand management becomes a bit excruciating.  Which events do you preclude?  Which do you play early to hope your opponent will burn through markers?  Which do you just bury in the debate pile?  I know, it sounds like it should be fun (and I usually like tough choices), but it's actually a bit exhausting to spend so much time thinking about how to avoid the bad things, rather than simply focusing on the positive.  

Other big concerns that ultimately led to the sale: (1) The game is at least one turn too long, and I usually end up sacrificing atmosphere (reading the cards and "experiencing" the events within an alternate-reality 1960) to save a little time.  (2) The fact that the game is a set length leads to a little too much back-and-forth time biding, as compared to Twilight Struggle which is always at risk of ending (and is more a game of momentum overall).  (3) I still don't understand the strategy behind the debates.  I'm starting to think I'm just hopeless on that side of things.  

Originally, I thought this would be one of my favorite games.  But repeated play didn't bear that out.  It felt too unsettling, too long, and too boring to really captivate me.  If someone invited me to play, I'd probably happily agree.  But it wasn't something that saw play often enough to justify its space on my shelf.

RATING: 8
[17/12/2007] I've now played more than 30 games. That alone indicates my regard for the new gold standard of political games.

That said, I feel the game's not perfect. Among experienced players, the early and mid-game tends to degenerate into an overly intense tug of war over the issues at the expense of other means of spending your resources. The Dunnagan variant solves that issue, in my opinion. With that variant, I'd happily give the game a 9.5.  

Why not even then an outright 10? Well, the bar I set for an outright 10 is exceedingly high. My view is that some of the card events aren't as attractive as I'd like them to be. But it's not a huge issue for me. Just one which puts the game half a point short of perfection (if including the Dunnagan variant).

----

Update 2014: I think I need to dock the game a few points. The game as published simply has a severe issue with the issues. But I still like it a great deal and it's really one-of-a-kind.

RATING: 4
It feels long. I suspect it's because we spent so much time fighting over New York, making the game feel repetitive. And if I'm going to play a long game, I want it to feel epic, not repetitive.

The second biggest flaw would be that two rest cubes doesn't balance out two extra campaign points (or a better event). We discovered that because I drew mostly fours and threes, while my opponent drew mostly twos and threes, and I walked away with the presidency. Those extra CPs meant I had momentum the entire game while my opponent never did, which let me use more events, which let me have a huge advantage.

Now, I'm sure that card distribution doesn't happen every game, but it really had my opponent slogging through nine rounds of an unwinnable game. It felt more like Bush vs. Dukakis than Nixon vs. Kennedy.

On the plus side, it certainly gave me an idea of why the electoral system in the US is so messed up. I mean, we only cared about big ticket states, content to pick up other ones as incidentals. So, if you weren't worth at least ten, we completely ignored you. Under twenty, and we were content to leave the leads established early until the end of the game, fighting only over states worth more than twenty.

RATING: 7.5
I tried this after Twilight Struggle and now prefer this. I rarely play 2p, but when I do, I would rather play this than TS as it’s much easier and faster to play. While the way you play cards for events or cubes is reminiscent of the gameplay of TS (and is something I’m fond of), and you do control some areas, the way you score points makes the game quite different: you play 8 rounds and tally up your points, and I think I prefer this to the VP track of TS. Also, there isn’t really any connection between the states in 1960 as opposed to the countries in TS, which has a big impact on the strategy.

Speaking of strategy and of winning the game, it does suck a little that NY/PA/CA have such a huge impact on who is winning. I’ve won all of my games, but if the balance had tipped in one of those states, the outcome would have been different in two of them. Yes, I know, it’s thematic, and you also fight a lot for those states as they are so important compared to e.g. Hawaii and the states in the West, but that just feels… odd. I don’t want to spend so much time fighting back and forth about a few key states! I know, if none of the players fight over them in the beginning, the game can open up a little, but it will always end with players trying to get control over those states, at least that has happened in my games. They just seem too important.

The issues is a good other way to spend your cubes, and while the endorsements and president markers are good, I don’t feel like they are overpowered at all as it’s not too hard to prevent the endorsements from getting your opponents a lot of points. I like that you can use your own markers for either following events or blocking your opponents from following events. The debate is a good idea and it can change the outcome of the game somewhat, though in the worst-case scenario you won’t lose too many cubes as you should be able to win at least one of the debates. Personally I feel like it’s better during the game to dump some very strong event cards (which your opponents could use) in the debate and then go for just winning one of the debates, but maybe that’s just me. I just know it’s worked every time so far with both candidates. 

It’s clever that you can control a state if you have 4 cubes in it so your opponent needs to make checks unless they control the media in that region. However, these checks feel a little random… And it sucks so much to lose 2-1 when drawing the starting player each round as you will almost always be chosen to go first which is almost always a disadvantage. Yes, of course, your opponent does lose some cubes from the bag, but still. Also, it’s quite random at the end when you don’t know in which four states your opponents make check… This sucks quite a lot, especially because the bag can run out of cubes and then it’s just tough luck (I know about the refill). 

But, anyway, I like the game a lot. I love that you’re forces to play cards which your opponent can follow (though you can prevent it if you have a couple of markers yourself), and it always feels like you’re fighting an uphill battle! It only takes a couple of hours to play, so while it isn’t as deep as Twilight Struggle, I’m going to play 1960 more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
A most excellent game.  This is all about playing the right cards at the right time, and in the correct order.  Everything else is just a bonus.  The fact that you can take advantage of a card played by your opponent makes the momentum aspect of the game very important.  There is the potential that the "Issues" portion of the game can be a little overpowering, but I think that only works against you if you decide to ignore that part of the game - at your detriment.  Expect to lose if you ignore the issues.

Overall, an excellent game, and one that I will try to get to the table as often as possible.

RATING: 5
The most disappointing game I have ever played. I don't understand how common opinion places this as the 11th best game of all time (on 2/3/08). Noone I know likes this game. It looks superficially like it will be a great game but in the end the events swing it too much. If you draw well, you do well; if you draw poorly, you do poorly. There's no feeling of control. I love the concept but the game feels like it could have done with a couple more years of development to make something tense and exciting.

RATING: 7
After one play, I liked it. I feel like you really need to embrace the chaos. But once you do, it is very enjoyable. What shocked me the most is how it really felt like an election. Anything could happen. One moment you're on offense, thinking you've got all the cards. Then all of a sudden the whole game has turned. One interesting thing about the cards is that you can play without knowing the cards and have a lot of fun. That is not the case with a lot of card driven games. Of course, not knowing the cards, you are liable to get thumped by someone who does. The only reason why this is a 8 and not an 9 after one play is the time it took. Definitely not the 1 1/2 hours on the box. Took more like 3. Super pretty package, great gameplay. Could go up to a 9 with more plays.

Update without even playing again: I'm not sure why I originally gave this an 8. I just haven't had the desire to play again. It just felt a touch too chaotic. Yes, like a real election, but not a particularly fun game. Maybe it'll go up if I do end up playing again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[UPDATE 1/16]: So I note that I have this ranked higher than Twilight Struggle, which seems like something worth justifying, because it's a pretty serious minority opinion. I am inclined to tip my cap to Twilight Struggle as a better designed and maybe more strategically interesting game-qua-game, so this is an instance of my personal tilt being a tiebreaker. 1960's theme is closer to my heart, of course, but even more so I think that the theme is better integrated with the mechanics of 1960 than TS. The gaminess of TS (which is at the heart of what makes TS a good game) does take me out of the feeling of re-creating history that a good historical wargame (or what[i]ever[/i]) should have. 1960 is also quicker and more accessible while still keeping a comparable level of exciting cut and thrust in the card play.

:sugar: :( :D Talk about your epic struggles between good and evil; I've played games in the role of the Soviet Union, al-Qaeda, Nazi Germany, and the Cali Cartel but never have I felt guiltier than when I looked my father in the eye and told him that I was replaying the 1960 election in the role of Richard Nixon. Such are the travails of life as a third-generation Irish-American yellow-dog Democrat.

Anyhoo.

1960, while not in the rarefied air of the most sublime card-driven games, is still a lot of fun to play and represents a nice distillation of the form into a game that smart people who are not well-versed in gaming will pick up with relative ease, not to mention a game that plays at a reasonable length of time for a weeknight. One problem with it, like most CDGs in which the cards are never (or almost never) reshuffled, is that the order in which the cards are dealt has a powerful effect on how easy or difficult the game is for one player or another. (A card that lets you fish the card of your choice out of the discard is inconsequential in your first hand, and massively powerful in the last third of the game, frex.) Twilight Struggle deals with this nicely by dividing the game into three mini-decks, two of which will see pretty much every card come out in a semi-predictable order.

In 1960, every card will be used (in some way) once and a few (but only a very few) will come out twice, so someone can do well and someone can be screwed by the luch of the draw.

And issue control is overpowered and media is underpowered. (Would that real life elections were this way.)

And there's a lot of zero-sum see-saws over key states and issues. 

And the support check mechanism, while cool, is underutilized and doesn't function as well as a catch-up as one might hope.

And all of these things are serious issues. But I have a lot of fun playing it, I love the theme, it's well produced and (at least before it went OOP) affordable, and once you learn it, you can play in 90 minutes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.1
This game is a card driven game a la Twilight Struggle with the 1960 Presidential Election as a theme.  While Twilight Struggle is closer to a wargame CDG, 1960 is more of a Euro CDG.  The game was co-designed with Jason Matthews and he brought over some ideas from Twilight Struggle (such as opponent events being playable during your turn).  After one play, this game is lots of fun, plays quickly, and doesn't seem to require the brain-melting analysis that Twilight Struggle sometimes demands.  An interesting theme with interesting mechanics.

RATING: 6
Fun game with straightforward rules, extremely well-themed and very nice components. I'm a big fan of Twilight Struggle, so its nice to see more political 2-player contests with similar mechanics coming out.Only going through the deck "about" once takes away some of the fun deck management see in TS, but its just a different game. I'll have to play more times to get a better sense of things, but at this point I prefer TS by at least 1 rating point. It seems there may be a "winning strategy" by focusing on the issues and trying to generate a momentum advantage. 

This game is not growing on me with repeated plays. I love the theme...and would like continue to see more new games in the genre (Twilight Struggle, etc), but this one just seems to be too luck sensitive. Anyway, it just feels like there is something about the game that turns me off...and detracts from the decision-making.

The person who wins initiative and forces the opponent to go 1st will usually be able to dominate the issues. 

Twilight Struggle has more of a learning curve, with the events and knowledge of the idiosyncracies of the board as well as card combinations...which ultimately makes it a much more satisfying game than 1960. Still, with how short 1960 is, I'd play it most any time someone requested it.

RATING: 6
I could not get my head around playing Nixon I could not even associate myself with the events on the cards.  I kept seeing what was good for JFK must be good full stop.  I missed opportunities simply because of my broken game radar, I been playing WW2 games for years and so I should be used to playing the role of an organisation/person I have no empathy with!  I kept on trying to play cards like I was JFK. 

I had two or three good turns at the beginning of this game and my opponents presence in the game almost vanished.  I've never seen anything like in a top end strategy game.  I then felt like I was going through a mincer for the rest of the game, bad decisions (I really didn't play well) and my opponents got a few breaks the situation turned around slowly.  

On the last ordinary turm I lost most of the highest value cities (New York etc.) and the opponents cards on the last turn scuppered my fight back completely.  I should of lost but I held most of the low scoring states and Texas and that was enough.  I played badly and lost most of the battles I was focused on and still won.  I think that Twilight Struggle gets accused of being a onesided luckfest but this can take the biscuit.  

I will in no doubt get my head around being Nixon and will play better next time (I really hate playing a game badly win or lose) but my opponent was robbed.  Those first two turns set me up for the rest of the game, my bad play and indifferent luck was not enough to change the outcome.

This is a Twilight like game that can be played in less than 3.5 hours (thematically bang on which as an ex wargammer matters a lot) and that is as far as I'm concerned game set and match.

2014
I have a suspicion that good play and victory in this game can be a tad disconnected.  I played badly and won the first game and hoped that this was a fluke. The second game produced the same kind of one sided colapse as the first game.  I felt powerless as the loosing player to change the outcome.

A game that takes over 2 hours to play and is this luck driven does not rock my world.  I will be revisiting this game at some point and hope I'm wrong.

2015 - 1989, Twilight Struggle  and Wir sind das Volk! all float my boat more.  Its the singer not the song.

RATING: 5
Remove the heavy theme/mechanic integration, rich tactical and strategic gameplay, and sheer design brilliance from TWILIGHT STRUGGLE and you're left with 1960: THE MAKING OF A PRESIDENT.  It's really a shame, because the theme and concept are staggeringly great and the overall feel of the game is very cool.  However, whereas TWILIGHT STRUGGLE was a sometimes subtle, sometimes brutal tug-of-war game 1960 turns out to be another appease-the-BGG-masses exercise in cube adjustment.  For all the excellent use of period photography and artwork, most of the cards simply adjust where cubes are between the board, the rest area, and supply.  Sorry, but to boil the really interesting issues and situations of the 1960 election to pushing cubes around doesn't make for an interesting game at all.  In sum, 1960 feels strangely abstract and the opacity of long-term objectives (unless you've memorized every state's electoral votes) coupled with the fact that you can play beautifully and then completely lose in the last three turns of the game make for a dull and unfortunately disengaging experience.  Of course, the BGG mindset holds that more abstraction is better, so I guess that explains why many are saying that it's "better" than TWILIGHT STRUGGLE.  It's not a total disaster- the theme, as it is, remains fascinating and it's a little more accessible (read: less wargamey) than TWILIGHT STRUGGLE. It's also shorter overall so it avoids being both long and boring.

RATING: 7
A good follow up to Twilight Struggle. The topic is just great! Who would have thought to make a board game based on that election? The production value is wonderful, much better than the treatment TS got with GMT Games. One small issue that I have though and that has been brought up in the forums is that there are not enough cubes in the game and you can run out. Z-Man should have included more cubes, so that this would not be a problem.

It is a lot lighter than TS, but that is fine, as it helps it to play quicker. I like the idea of drawing cubes out of the bag to determine things like initiative and being to control how the cubes go into the bag based on the cards you play. The lower the CP value of the card the more you can seed the bag with your color cubes and thus shift the odds more into your favor. 

In the end I do prefer TS to this though, as I find TS to be a much richer experience and I prefer the cold war topic a little more to the election one. Still this is an excellent game and I am happy it is on my game shelf.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
The more I play games with 'battle cards', the more I realise I'm not cut out for them. They're usually too weak on actual game, and too swingy in how they play out. 1960 is no exception: The incessant back-and-forth over states becomes grating after a far too short while; and because of the game's zero sum-nature it really becomes a matter of deciding how to deal with the Big League states first, and then how to hurt the outlook of the opponent as cheaply as possible (using media and/or endorsements, the latter via Issues) in all the little ones. (That is not to say that well-timed media pushes are without their use in the Big League states: they can be hellishly annoying, in fact.) This is simply the effect of efficiency of actions (which are obviously limited): in Big League states this efficiency is way higher than in Backwater Hick states. And because this strategy will ultimately be the same for every game, matters will soon turn to how the events fall. Which is, at best, a semi-random process. I just find it not fun, even annoying, to play my cards in a specific order to maximise on their potential in one game turn, only to have that effort being nearly completely undone in the next (replacing it with a slightly altered game state) through no fault of my own. Why not just roll a D6 and be done with it in a fraction of the time?

These games work far better as simulations of their chosen topic rather than as actual games, I find. As such they serve well as primers which I may or may not delve further into. 1960 is really of limited interest to me: it's not part of [i]my[/i] history. And that quells further exploration of both topic and game, such as there is. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Theme       -> :star::star::star::star:
Down Time   -> :star::star::star::nostar:
[/c]

I tend to find myself playing the theme rather than the game, but that is alright because that is what you need to do.

A little down time when you first start as you have to read the cards and get adjusted, but after that it seems to move pretty fast.

RATING: 3
[b]Thumbs Down[/b] after one game. We found the impacts of debate to be too weak (even if you invest lightly, and your opponent heavily, the odds are very high that you can force no worse than a 7-2 loss... and a 5-cube difference will be more than offset by the better cards you played on the board). I would be inclined to then have 3 of the 5 cards be my opponent's biggest events buried, which lessens the impact of momentum (which already seems weak). This in turn weakens issues. 

So, what's left to actually care about is the tit-for-tat influence struggle, which is really simplistic and boring. Also, it seems some events are just really killer windfalls.

Objectively speaking, it's a very interesting design with great chrome, but that's not enough for me to make up for a dull game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Outstanding game by Christian Leonhard and Jason Matthews, the Designer of [gameid=12333]. So many layers of interest, brilliantly meshed together to create a truly nail-biting experience. Each card from your hand can be used as Campaign Points to gain favor in a particular region (4 total regions, across the USA map), move to another region, gain media coverage in a region (which makes takeovers easier), gain Endorsements (which wins all undecided states in a region, during the last round election), to gain points in issues (points in Civil Rights, Defense, and Economy gain momentum and/or endorsements), or as an Event. The Events are historical events that happened during the election and they are played out in game terms (such as adding or removing campaign cubes to and from certain states or regions). The catch is that when you use a card for something other than an Event, the opposing player can hijack the event for himself and use it against you! This design is so solid and approachable, I think it could appeal to almost any gaming audience. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Looking at the components, reading the rules and playing through half a game for familiarity I had hopes that this may be a 9  game.

However after playing, albeit only once, I'm not so sure. It is a good game and definitely one of the best mechanics to theme fits I have played. However it is subject to a large amount of luck in terms of the card draw - I'm hoping that over 7 hands of cards this will normally average out to a closer game, however in the first game I played it did not. On 3 turns out of 7 I was playing with approx 12CP v 18CP and on two of those turns had events which primarily favoured my oponent whilst he was able to hit with several uber event cards that allowed placing of 5-7 state control. Two of the debates were tied therefore going to the player with the initiative which is decided on a cube pull from the bag. My oponent played a solid game but there was no way that I could have won this.

I still enjoyed the game as the mechanics and theme are great but I am concerned that it can be beset by a degree of luck that can skew the game so much. Looking forward to playing again but with fingers crossed that the first playing was just a series of freaky deals.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Players represent Nixon and Kennedy fighting out the 1960 US presidential campaign.  Essentially a card driven wargame combined with a Die Macher-esque competition for US state seats.  The seats are worth different amounts of electoral votes, ranging from 45 down to 3, and players have to claim around 270 votes to win.  Cards are played to sprinkle influence cubes across the states, media and major issues (defence, economy, civil rights).  Some neat ideas, including the "rest cup" to replenish cubes, momentum to trigger/deny events, the debate midway through the game, as well as the final election where swings are still possible.  Neat, well developed game, that seems geared to providing exciting finishes.  A few bits feel clunky, but overall quite a solid game.  Fantastic presentation, components are of excellent quality too.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Updated now that I have played the published game:  This is a lean card-driven influence game. It is similar to Twilight Struggle but with only a fraction of the rules overhead. It has a great new innovation to triggering events in the game. If an opponent's card has an t want, it might or might not happen depending on whether you want to the spend precious momentum tokens. But your opponent can also spend to affect this as well... a great new twist to the system.  Also in the mix is a clever interaction between regional advertising, dominating the issues, and framing the relative importance of the issues. These are all great fun and make the whole thing an engaging, interlocking series of give-or-take decisions. My rating reflects my dislike of the fact that card draws trump board play. Highly tactical and you are just reacting to complicated cards. I felt this was a slight problem in Twilight Struggle, but here it is more pronounced. If this  didn't bother you about TS, then there's no reason why you wouldn't like 1960 even more than TS.  The production, art, and presentation are absolutely top-notch, kudos to Z-Man and the design team!

RATING: 7
I finally played a thoroughly enjoyable game of 1960.  There were many cards used for the events and many used for the CP.  Game was pretty close the whole time from my point of view with no player ever being ahead by more than 100 or so.  For once I felt the tension!  I've played this game about 5 times now and can highly recommend it.

As noted by others, very similar to Twilight Struggle.  I really like the theme in this game.  Very unique compared to other heavy 2-player titles.  Also not super long, with a usual game clocking in around 2 hours.  There are some aspects of Twilight Struggle that make it a better game than this one in my opinion.  Those aspects primarily being the midgame scoring, and mid/late game card additions to the deck.  On the other hand I feel the lack of dice in 1960, and the inclusion of the political capital bag were very nice additions that made the game feel less luck-based.  Also somewhat annoying in 1960 is how you can preempt your opponents events, whereas in TS there is no way to stop opponents events from happening (except by discarding/keeping the card).

RATING: 4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Nice design. Although I enjoy Twilight Struggle, I think 1960 solves a number of small issues I have with it.

1) Bad hands (CP-wise) are mitigated by Rest Cubes and Campaign Bag.
2) Bad hands (event-wise) are mitigated by Momentum.
3) More things to juggle (media, endorsements, issues, debates).
4) Scoring only happens at the end.

It's a simple system made complex by 91 unique events. Each card has 4 functions: event (for one or both players), campaign points (campaign, advertise, position), issue icon for debates, and state for election day. Each full game turn, you have either 6 or 7 cards. You need to decide which cards to play during the turn, and which you want to save for the debate/election day. For the debate, you generally want high-valued cards with your own icon and a symbol for the issue(s) you want to win. For election day, you want cards with states that have a chance of swinging your way with 3 support checks. In both these cases, you don't know exactly what you might need at the beginning of the turn (and less so in earlier turns), and you may even change your mind as the events play out.

---

I have changed my mind on this. After playing it a bit, I now understand why TS works the way it does, and I enjoy it more.

RATING: 7
An interesting take on the asymmetrical 2-player system, popularized by Twilight Struggle. The political twist lends itself to a lighter tone than Twilight Struggle, somewhat ironically, with much of the historical context written in as flavor text on the cards.
The most interesting thing about this game is the end-game scoring, which borrows from many euro-style games. The game is divided into the weeks leading up to the election, culminating, like the real thing, with a tallying of the electoral votes. While this method of scoring is sometimes frowned upon as anti-climatic, it lends itself well to the theme creating an intense Election Day showdown.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I have only played this game twice so far, and I'm absolutely loving it!  It takes a while to explain and setup (a lot of pieces & tokens to place), along with a considerable amount of rules to learn.  But eventually it all clicks.  It's a neat blend of area control, bluffing, and a slick card-driven system that allows players many options in playing their hands.  It's exciting to see how a particular move can have deep "ripple effects" that impact other layers.

I haven't played Twilight Struggle yet, but from what I hear it's even better than 1960:TMofP (but also longer).  Until then, I must say this is an outstanding game as it provides a highly engrossing experience.  Both games took nearly 3 hours (with setup & rules explanation).  Even so, the time flew on by.  It's really a treat to have this much FUN, TENSION, and AGONIZING choices tightly woven in a 2-player game.  If there is anything I can remotely complain about, there *MAY* be a slight clunkiness in how the Debate Phase works.  Perhaps it's there to help fit the election theme?  Or maybe this "clunkiness" is just my imagination.

I also love the tension of building the campaign strategy decks.  It's not only a great way to get rid of unfavorable cards (like the Church in San Juan), but it also accelerates the tension.  Both the debate and election phases are great "battles" where players run like mad attempting to push states in their favor before the final election.

Excellent job Jason & Chris!  Grade: A

RATING: 7
I don't think I've ever played a CDG for the first time and not had any rules questions.  It's very clear with excellent rules.  I have a strong bias towards this game due to my enjoyment of Twilight Struggle, and so far 1960 gets close to the mark.  It's as boiled down as a card driven game with events can get and It gives a clean euro feel with all the theme of the events and card photos.  I like the fact that with a card like 'Nixons knee' for example, you're only moving your token to Maryland for the event but the graphic on the card and the text create a story about why Nixon was in Maryland and what the knee injury meant to his campaign.  It's a tight look at a year in history and it really draws me in.  I'm a political cartoonist and a history buff and this game is right up my alley.    Plays in about two hours and will teach you something about elections.  Very high level of decision stress for both players as well.  I hope we see expansions for this game to include other close elections(Truman/Dewey 1948).

Update:   Lowering my rating from a 9 to an 8.  I'm not sure how much it matters what you do in this game.  The system seems to go back and forth across the board by itself.  It has a feeling that whatever I do on my turn, you will undo on your turn.  In the end it's fun but I never feel like I'm in control or choosing a strategy.  I guess it's all tactical and not strategic.

RATING: 6
While it's not as engrossing as Twilight Struggle, it's shorter. I love the election day support checks.  They give the feel of waiting around on election day to see if there are going to be any big upsets.  Definitely use the variant for placing cubes into the issues.

Unfortunately, after many plays I feel like all the cards are sort of the same.  In Twilight struggle there's just a wider variety of things that can happen because you're awarded points throughout the game and because the adjacency rules for influence, placement can make any country crucial at some point in the game; timing matters.  The gathering momentum cards just don't have half the tension or meaning that the scoring cards do in TS.  Still, 1960 is an amazing game, it just doesn't have that "it" factor that makes me want to play it more the more I play.

RATING: 6
This is an all-right game, but the gameplay is definitely missing something for me.  For example, one way TS introduced tension was due to significant bottlenecks on its map: you had to get there before the opponent, else your game became a lot harder; here there are very few such tension-inducing bottlenecks.  Comments proclaiming it the "best election game EVAR" seem overhyped to me.  The first five rounds are very "samey"-feeling as cubes are placed about the board; round 6 breaks the pattern -- but only to place a further four-five cubes -- then it's back to the same old thing again before some cube-twiddling in the election round.  In the end it feels like a three-turn game that takes ten turns to play.

RATING: 7
I'm not totally sure what to make of this. As an object it's wonderful, of course: massive great board, all this electoral paraphernalia, flavour text explaining what the cards are about on the actual cards (instead of relegated to the booklet like Twilight Struggle). As a game... well, I do enjoy it, I've played it twelve times now, but its tug-of-war, zero-sum nature means there's not all that much palpable sense of progress, means the board state has a lot of toing and froing, and the fact that so many events alter state support means the already pretty minor sense of geography that dividing the board into regions gives is further undermined. But I do enjoy it, it's got a great theme and implements it well. I dunno, maybe it's just not a theme that lends itself to a game I'll love.

RATING: 4
02/22/08--Solid.  Wonder how it would hold up to heavy repeat plays...not sure it would fare too well.  There really don't seem to be that many "game changers" in terms of events, not to a degree where a particular game would vary wildly from another.  Nice effort, I really want to play Twilight Struggle now.

EDIT:  After you play it more than once, you find it out it's the same tug-of-war game after game.  It's too easy to undo what your opponent does, very much unlike Twilight Struggle where that's often simply not possible to easily undo something immediately after.  It makes the gameplay stale.

The issues are busted, and suffer from the same tug-of-war.  Worse because geography isn't even a factor.

You would think "carrying" a state would help against this, but there are so many cards that directly add influence and bypass those bag checks completely.  There's no hurry to get them; you don't score until the end, and those influence cards will come around eventually.  Until then, just put out the fires that are the easiest and cheapest to deal with.

I like the theme a lot.  I like the art direction and the cards.  But playing the game itself?  Literally, this is like a three-play and done game.  Shame, I wanted to like this one.

RATING: 7
1960 offers a strong campaign simulation for two players, as you vie for electoral control by influencing media, spinning hot debate issues, and good old fashioned hob-nobbing with the people.  This is of course abstracted through card play and cube placement, but 1960 does a strong job of fastening itself to the applied theme.  All decisions are of vast importance determining best strategies and also tactically responding to your opponents moves.  1960 offers a tension filled couple of hours, usually in close contention, until the final count.  Wonderfully produced by Z-man, elevating it above other games in the genre for me.  Also the theme is not tired, and offers a great change from the usual renaissance, viking, and ancient civilization themes that have all been beaten to death and then some.

RATING: 6
This is a good game, but I miss the subtlety, the nervous tension, the sharper focus, and the much greater level of planning that goes into Twilight Struggle.  1960 is much closer to a so-called "Eurogame," so I suspect it will have broader appeal.  Having played a lot of Twilight Struggle, 1960 comes across as a bit bland in comparison.  There's not much to distinguish the states except for their number of electoral votes.  Consequently, you're mostly just trying to drop as many cubes into as many high-value states as possible, and ignoring the less efficient, smaller states unless you can gain a bunch of small-state cubes in one broad stroke.  As a result, every turn feels very much the same as the previous turn.  The core card play system is so enjoyable, though, that the game is worth playing despite my disappointment with the board play.  The game's best new feature is the concept of momentum, a resource that can be spent to activate favorable events on your opponent's cards.  This works very well and generates difficult decisions.  A momentum advantage can be very powerful in the early and middle portions of the game, although it becomes less important in the final two turns when it's easier to bury your opponent's most powerful events.

One play. I enjoyed it, though I'm not 100% sold on it yet. I agree with a lot of the stuff that Chris Farrell has said in his comments; there's stuff in there that looks good on paper but doesn't add much to the game (the media, the advantage of "carrying" a state). RATING: 6.5
Lacks the depth of Twilight Struggle but it is more accessible, quicker to play, and requires fewer rules checks over the course of a game. I like the theme at least as much as in Twilight Struggle, although I can see where that almost certainly won't be the case for a world-wide audience. I will probably burn out on this and then return to Twilight, but it promises to be a fun ride. At minimum, worth bringing out once a year or so. Very handsome production values, too -- on a physical quality level I'm hard-pressed to identify a better-produced game in my library. I think keeping a running tally of electoral votes is probably critical beginning with the debates turn, otherwise the endgame will seem even more chaotic and arbitrary. The game does generate a narrative (Nixon steals the election with a re-count in Texas!), and the opportunity for puns and jokes abound if you know even a little bit of history. And if you don't know your history, you can still say, "Dick, you don't know Jack!" A winner.

RATING: 7
1960 : The Making of the President is a good 2 player game where each player try to become the US President.
You have a set of cards that you can play as an Event or use the Campaign point.  These Campaign point can be use to :
- Do campaign in a state
- Advertising in a region
- Positioning on issues

There is also a debate and the election day.
The player who have the more votes after the election day wins the game.

RATING: 10

RATING: 7.75
Initial rating of 8

The theme doesn't do much for me. I tend to not like games with a political theme, not to mention that this period of history is not all that thrilling to me. However, this game really is quite good.

Q: So, you don't like politics as a theme AND the history is not interesting to you, yet you rate this game above a 7?

A: Yes, I do. The mechanics of this game are very well done. Card driven area majority with plenty of circumstantial card play. 

One of the key elements is to recognize how each hand is best played and yet allow yourself some flexibility to react to your opponents card play. Many subtle factors come into play and are only learned through experience.

Q: These subtle factors, are they really difficult to grok? 

A: Probably for the first two or three plays, unless you are a CCG or card driven kind of person. I would suggest watching a few rounds and asking questions then trying it out with another noob or someone that will actually teach and explain what is happening during the course of your first play instead of feasting on your new blood.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
1960: The Making of the President uses several of the same mechanisms found in Matthews' earlier design, Twilight Struggle.  The card-driven aspect, wherein cards can be used in a variety of manners, is very similar.  This is an extremely appealing mechanism, as it forces players to make vital decisions with the play of each and every card.  The tenseness and angst caused during this decision making process is considerable and, for me, the best part of the game.  Considerable research and care has been performed to insure that the cards have historical connections and reflect the ebb and flow of a presidential campaign.  The campaign deck seems well balanced, and the availability of the momentum markers allows players to trigger favorable events that may have escaped their use due to any "luck of the draw" occurrences.  There doesn't appear to be any undue built-in advantage for a particular candidate.

The game is certainly lighter and easier-to-understand than Twilight Struggle.   Twilight Struggle is a wonderful, tense and exciting game, but it is a bit rules-heavy with lots of detail and occasionally confusing situations.  It is not a game for the faint-of-heart.  1960: The Making of the President is considerably easier to learn and play, making it accessible for a wider audience.  Granted, the subject matter might not be as popular outside of the United States or appeal to a younger age group who may not have any interest in past presidential elections, but those willing to give it a try will find it far easier to become acclimated.   

Another advantage is that 1960 plays quicker than Twilight Struggle.  Most games can be played to completion in ninety minutes or so, and there is less downtime.  It is more straight-forward and less confusing, all factors that help the game move along at a brisk pace.  

That being said, the game does not appear to have the strategic depth of Twilight Struggle.  Plus, the board situation can and often does change dramatically.  While there are steps players can take to protect themselves and increase the odds in their favor, there is still a healthy dose of luck present, especially during the support checks.   The final shake-up caused by the rectifying of the campaign cards players have set aside during the final two turns can be dramatic and may actually swing the election.  While these support checks can be fun and exciting, they can also be frustrating as they are heavily dependent upon luck.  That may be distasteful to some.

While Twilight Struggle remains my favorite of the two designs (I'm a bit biased, though, as my Master's Degree concentration was World War II and the Cold War!), I find myself playing 1960: The Making of the President more often.  This is predominately due to the length of the game and the ease in teaching it to others.  It is a fun game that really does capture some of the atmosphere, excitement and uncertainties of a presidential campaign.  Surprisingly, given the nearly universal recognition and appeal of the subject, there are far too few good election games available.  Fortunately, 1960: The Making of the President is one of those few very good ones.
