=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Note: My rating is for the Rules as Written.  You can easily house rule this up to an 8 or so.  

Overrated here on the geek, but does capture a lot of the CDG feel in a pretty short time frame.  Similar to Twilight Struggle, but lighter, shorter, and more euro-influenced. I do not like it as well as TS or HRVC and that is because I felt less in control of my own destiny.  The decisions are just easier in 1960.  Also, I think it has to do with the huge discrepancies of the electoral vote totals vs TS's more unpredictable scoring.  At a couple points during the game, I thought it should have been named 1960: the making of NY, PA, TX, Ill, CA, and a handful of side battles.  Its a solid game and perhaps all of the finer points haven't come out yet.Update: another play, and some of my lingering concerns are still lingering!  The game does not have the tension of Twilight struggle because you know when the scoring is going to happen.  The board geography is meaningless because its so cheap to move your candidate around.  The whole "control" mechanism where you can lock up a state is pretty meaningless because it doesn't effect event card play.  Advertising is heavily overcosted because you can break a "carried" state with events so easily.  The Debates are weak, and strategically, you gain a cube advantage by ignoring them most of the time.  Overall, the decisions just aren't really that interesting, you just add or subtract cubes for 90 minutes then count up the scores.  Nearly every hand you draw, you will have the same very basic decisions to make.  There will be a couple of really good events that will net you more cubes for the event than the CPs, there will be a couple of weaker ones that don't, or that aren't that good because of the situation on the board.  I did finally experience the "issue war" problem people have seen with repeated plays.  Very frustrating, and shows the problems with a lot of the side mechanisms in the game.  Easily fixed with a house rule, but obnoxious nonetheless.The game feels like it plays itself once you catch on to the optimization strategies with the cubes and control of different states.  In its attempt to be like a "tightly designed euro" 1960 took out all the tension and interesting decisions Twilight Struggle fans were hoping to get in a shorter package.  The game is so tightly designed and many of the decisions are so automatic that you will find a lot of games between good players are won by just a handful of votes.  Often, a lucky or unlucky support check at the end of the game, or one player forgetting about an election event can swing it.  This sort of designer-manufactured-tension is great, but won't play for twilight struggle fans.  We are used to the real thing!  The game is so close to being great, but so many of the mechanics and card interactions really contradict each other and encourage boring play.  I know thats the "balance" and "elegance" that eurogames seek to achieve and this was supposed to be the euro-fied CDG, but I'd like to tweak the game a little to fix these things.

RATING: 3
If randomness was a serious problem in Twilight Struggle, it is bluntly lethal in this one.

Some events are way too powerful. And if you get lucky drawing the right cards when your opponent does not during a turn or two, your advantage can turn into a mountain impossible to climb.

Besides, there's little than someone can do to take advantage of your own candidate's most powerful events if 
they fall in your opponent's hands.

The black bag reminds me of the now-so-famous Shogun tower and its "impredictible" results.

And last but not least, all the thematic details as media support, issue support, endorsements or the debates (what a lamey depiction of the latter!) seem to be pretty much worthless. You are better off by focusing on placing as many cubes as you can in the most meaningful states and forget about the rest of the game.

All in all, a completely overhyped and overrated game that can be fun once in a while if you enjoy moving cubes all over the board.

Liked the cards, though. But this game does not deliver the thrill of being a candidate in an election campaign at all.

RATING: 5
The follow up game to Twilight Struggle manages to be superior to its predecessor in its production values and reduced playing time, but it's weaker everywhere else. Like all single deck CDGs it's very random and swingy, but on top of that there's a lot less to really think about than meets the eye. There are plenty of false choices attempting to distract you from this simple purpose: Media is rarely worth investing in and the West is rarely worth campaigning in, but once these are ignored the player drawing the higher value cards will inevitably triumph. Initially I loved this for its political theme, but after 3 plays I felt I'd seen everything that was on offer here.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Excellent game on one of the most interesting US presidential elections.  Strange title aside, this game has almost everything.  Lots of fun, interesting gameplay options, and fairly strong history.  Players take turns playing cards, which can be used to send their candidates campaigning, purchase advertising, position on issues, or trigger historical events.  The events bring out an enormous amount of historical flavor and are well integrated into the gameplay for the most part, although a couple of the events that historically were crippling to Nixon don't seem all that powerful here (his knee injury and his pledge to visit all fifty states).  There is some debate as to the efficacy of advertising and the power of issue positioning.  I find the former to be fine but the latter to be a bit overpowered.  A small variant of prohibiting using cards to place positioning cubes on the issue denoted on the card has solved this problem for me.

The only major knock against the game is that it only covers one election.  I'd like to see a 1968 expansion, since it could reuse the same gameboard.  That election is interesting in that it could work quite well as either a two or three player game.

RATING: 7
Man, I don't think I've ever played a game that left me quite as frustrated (even days later) as 1960.  Granted, this is because I would have won the election if not for the Election cards that lost me control of California and Illinois (I played as Nixon), either of which would have given me the victory (fairly close to real life I suppose...). I obviously underestimated the power of the election cards and didn't realize there were only 3 of them in the deck, so my strategy would be quite different in the future (I had both the Kennedy Election cards in my hand and definitely would have sacrificed them in the debate if I had realized how important 5 support checks are during the election).  I think a knowledge of the deck is necessary to play 1960 effectively, perhaps even more so than Twilight Struggle since the cards are much more powerful in 1960 and many of them are focused on a few battleground states.  

The game is an interesting variation of the Twilight Struggle mechanics, but I couldn't help thinking during the game that I would rather be playing Twilight Struggle.  1960 seems much more random to me, with wild swings back and forth and the events weighted towards the high-value states (thematically relevant perhaps, but still frustrating to focus so much on one state only to see your efforts wiped out with an opponents' card).  I prefer Twilight's Struggle's limitations in terms of influence placement - to me it requires much more careful planning and actually increases the tension as you try to plan several moves ahead to accomplish your objective.  1960 feels much more rushed and chaotic - again, thematically appropriate, but to me not quite as satisfying.

I'll have to give this one another try - hopefully another go will get rid of the slightly bitter taste in my mouth.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
1st played 12/11/07. Taught ~15min, played ~2hrs. Probably speeds up a bit after acquainted w/ cards. Players are presidential candidates Nixon/Kennedy. Players start off with pre-set amount of % support (shown as cubes; empty is 50/50) from certain state (some are split/empty). Your job to campaign, advertise, gain endorsements, & debate to win over votes. Board is 4 regions and requires a campaign point(CP) to move one adjacent region. A marker shows your location. Cards have 2-4CPs on them which you use to place a cube(s) in a state(s), poll for endorsements, or advertise in a region. For polling, you draw cubes from the bag of cubes which gets replenished from the rest area. A 2CP card rewards 2 rest cubes. (cards add up to 4) Cards also have a special action listed which you could do instead. Example: own majority in a region, place one cube on each unoccupied state. If it benefits your opponent then he can play a momentum chip (MC) to do that action. Playing two MCs block him. End of each round the issue track (of 3) is evaluated with the leader getting endorsements and/or MCs depending on the priority of each. A debate for support in round 6. Most support wins. A bit long for me however it held my interest throughout. I'd recommend giving it a game play to decide.

Yes, it does take 1.5-2 hours, but it really isn't that complicated.  It does seem to have a lot in common with CC:E in that you tend to hand on for a roller coaster ride due to the event cards.  They can cause pretty large swings with a single card.

For our first two games, he who ruled the issues ruled the game.  If the players balanced the issues a bit more I'm sure the game would have been a bit closer.

The only real strikes against it are:

My wife doesn't like the theme at all.
It's still just a large game of "tug-of-war" underneath, and I don't particularly enjoy that feeling.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Unfortunately after playing Twilight Struggle this game falls flat.  Hand management is barely required; it is too easy to just bury bad events and never to have to worry about them again.

The tug of war on the issues is a boaring mechanic that can dominate the game.  An experienced player can win by just spending his actions in the issues.  If the other player responds in kind, then the game is spent mainly going back and forth with an I add/ I take away back forth playing of cards.  If the other player does not respond, then he will find himself in a pit that he can never dig himself out of.

Also, some of the events are just not well balanced.  One that comes to mind is the late congressional session.  If played on the last turn, the Kennedy player might as well just pack up his game and go home.

These flaws make this game far inferior to Twilight Struggle.  I think that there was good potential in this game, it just did not quite achieve what it could have.

RATING: 8
Better components than Twilight Struggle, but the gameplay isn't as compelling.  There seem to be fewer DIFFICULT choices.  Because of the open nature of laying campaign influence you actually have more options per turn, but at any given point in time most are obviously inferior choices.  Depending on how the cards deal out momentum markers (and thus, the issues subgame) can be very important or an almost non-factor.  Generally, the game just lacks the urgency that adds to the tension and fun of Twilight Struggle.

While it seems a bit weak compared to its sibling Twilight Struggle, this is still one of the best game releases of 2007 in my opinion.

RATING: 9.5
Play with all three variants:
1.  CPs spent on the Issues cannot be spent on the Issue icon on the bottom of the card.
2.  Whichever player has Advertising support in the most REGIONS, may switch the position of two adjacent Issues on the Issue track, BEFORE the Issues bonuses are resolved.  If there is a tie in regional Advertising support, there is no pre-bonus switching.  The player with the most total Advertising SUPPORT may still rearrange the positions of the Issue track AFTER the Issue bonuses are resolved.
3.  The threshold for Carrying a state is reduced from 4 cubes to 3 cubes.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Extremely interesting theme to me.  

Two distinct phases combine Twilight Struggle hand management pre-debate with more aggressive card play post-debate (since one can bury opponent symbols with more impunity if they are an appropriate state).

Randomness makes the game more replayable, but since the entire deck is generally used, the randomness is in when cards are played (and by whom), not whether they are played.  It's more of a balanced chaos and emblematic of how I feel randomness should be used in games -- not as a club to hand victory to one player, but as a game element to be managed along with other game elements like resources.

Two sides have distinctly different characters, but are balanced against each other.

August 2012 - I really hate the debate mechanic.  I hated it back four years ago too but I'm less willing to overlook it now.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
1960: The Making of the President takes the card driven system in Twilight Struggle and paints a political theme over it. the game is set in 1960 (duh) and features the presidential (duh) election between John F. Kennedy and Richard M. Nixon, where one man will be made (duh) president, while the other will become president another time (maybe in 1969?).

Similar to Twilight Struggle, the game uses cards that can be used as either Events or Campaign Points. The game does not contain a 'sudden death' mechanic ala Twilight Struggle, but instead has a fixed endpoint, which is the presidential election itself.

While I may have only played one game (so far), 1960 feels less tense and less unpredictable compared to Twilight Struggle. The theme is also less tense (no DEFCON) and the plays feel less punishing. Ultimately, it depends on the level of intensity that you want in your 2-player gaming sessions. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

One of the reasons I like Twilight Struggle is how the game recreates the paranoia and mindset of the Cold War.  This game does the same thing for a Presidential Election, desperation at being pulled in so many directions, taking your eye off of the issues to focus on a particular state, the cost of criss-crossing the country.  OK, I've never actually run for President, so I can't say it feels 100% accurate, but it is how I imagine it would feel.  Bravo on a sophomore effort from Jason and a freshman effort from Christian (both of whom I met at the 2007 WBC, and seemed very nice).  EDIT: After 3 plays my opinion of the game has only risen.  I'm having a hard time determining which is better, TS or 1960.  While clearly related, they are very different games.  The production values of the game are superb, IMnsHO.  I've heard people complain about the tit-for-tat aspect, and the "just wait for the right events to drop in you lap" strategy, but I haven't found either of those things in my handful of plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A decent game borrowing concepts from the designer's previous game Twilight Struggle. Card play requires decisions about how to maximize effects--do you play actions or spend Campaign Points to shore up states, advance the issues, etc. One of the major weaknesses is the Debate Turn. It feels really clunky, is difficult to explain, and seems overwrought for not enough reward--most times you are only going to net a +2 or +3 cube gain over your opponent. 

Momentum is arguably more useful just for its threat than for actually expending it. Players have to juggle their card sequence to negate the possible impact of opponents triggering events. 

There's a lot that will resonate with political junkies and enough meat to keep coming back to play periodically. I've taught this to a couple different people and they enjoyed it. But it's hard to escape the feeling that it's mostly a tit-for-tat battle over the 4 or 5 states with the biggest electoral vote values. 

The game is lavishly produced and a real pleasure to behold when all laid out and set up.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm really enjoying my playings of this so far. I like that the game play is in rounds. By this I mean that cards cannot be carried forward into future rounds. The game play is interesting and moves along at a good pace once the players are familiar with what they can do. The games I've played have been close and you could see where a move here or there could have made the difference.

RATING: 7
I can't help but compare this game to Twilight Struggle, a game I have mixed feelings about. TS's scoring is by arbitrary card draw and leads to over-focusing in a scoring region and then under-focusing in that same region after the card has been played. 1960 does not score until game's end and every region scores at the same time. This gives 1960 a much better arc than its predecessor, demonstrates an appreciation for the entire sphere of influences, and is an overall more satisfying game experience. It may be less complex than TS, but the mechanics make a lot more sense, both in game terms and thematically as well. 

My only concern is how well this game would hold up to repeated play. I do think TS has a significant edge over 1960 in the replayability department.

UPDATE: Sure enough, the replayability was slim. Traded 1960 while I still have TS. Go figure.

RATING: 3
Consistently irritating, win lose or draw. This is a game I would refuse to play under most circumstances. 

The gameplay falls flat on its face for a simple reason: influence is zero-sum / tit-for-tat. Entire turns can be flushed down the toilet undoing and re-doing identical moves. It is utterly tedious. Beyond that, there are three secondary failings. First, the "debates" are poorly integrated. You're supposed to save up your good cards (thematically) for them, but in practice, you just pitch your bad cards, because the rewards of winning the debates pale in comparison to the rewards for pitching the worst of your opponent's events (and deliberately losing the debates as a result). Second, there's no scoring at all until the end, which just intensifies the slogging it takes to get there. I don't even see this as terribly thematic. Mr. Matthews says it makes the game more strategic and less tactical, and I admire that in theory, but to my eyes the zero-sum nature of influence heavily undermines the potential for long-term play. Third, the events are about as subtle as a sledgehammer, and unexciting to boot. They don't give you interesting choices, and they don't create interesting effects within the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I enjoyed the desicions that need to be made with the cards during the game.  The theme makes this game engaging and I always feel like I am enjoying this game even when I am loosing the election in a landslide.  I would highly recommend this game.

RATING: 4
This is a great two player game, if you want to less tension than Twilight Struggle, but like the idea of TS.  I prefer TS, but am happy to own this b/c I feel it's more spouse friendly.  

5/1/2010:  i've downgraded it from 8 to 6.5 now that i've given TS a real workout in the last couple months.  TS is superior in every way, and not really all that much tougher for n00bs to pick up than 1960.  key states look like tugs of war in this game, the restrictions are gotten around too easily (control superceded by ads, mobility doesn't matter b/c you concentrate on one or two areas).  finally i don't like that there's just one scoring and that it's a bit hidden.  it makes for either a) calculators throughout the game, which doesn't work if you're trying to sell it to non-gamers or b) educated guesses about your status, which is just lame.  

9/5/2010 traded for Combat Commander Europe.

RATING: 5
Very favorable initial impression.  I like the play time, which I anticipate would come down with further play.  I really like the theme, and the graphic design is pleasing.  The gameplay feels similar to Twilight Struggle, and it's fun to try and make the best of what you are dealt.  Ultimately the luck of the draw plays a huge role, but I'm convinced that skilled players can overcome it in most cases.  I would really like to try to explore different strategies.  Election games are probably the biggest untapped mine of game ideas - I would love to see some more.

Update: After a couple more plays, I can say with authority that one must not ignore the issues.  It's just a little box over in the corner, but the endorsements are huge.  In fact, I'm not sure how to actually play the game now.  If my opponent was just focusing on the issues, I don't see how I can do anything else other than also concentrate there until our attention goes elsewhere.

Update: After playing Campaign Manager 2008, I have to downgrade 1960, as I enjoy CM2008 more.  It's shorter, less frustrating and repetitive (you add two cubes, I remove them), and the election is easier to relate to.

RATING: 7
A tug-of-war game about the most (famous?) American election. The game is pretty hard to rate because players can't tell how well they are doing throughout the game. Its almost like watching a story unfold which you have no control over. You play the cards and let the events take shape.

I am not convinced that there is much skillful play involved (aside from the timing of cards), but it is enjoyable to watch the election play out. The debates felt very out of place, almost like blind bidding for an auction. That aside, the game is definitely an improvement over Twilight Struggle.

RATING: 7
It's a less rulesy version of Twilight Struggle, with a lot of the same mechanics and so much simpler that it takes about two hours fewer to play and far less time to explain. What it loses in richness from TS it more than gains in smoothness. Card draws can affect play quite a bit and it's easy to underestimate the importance of Momentum tiles (I kept thinking of the strategic initiative tiles in Europe Engulfed...). I hear y'all who think the results of the debates can be a little arbitrary, but the cube swings actually aren't that bad; nine at most. Overall, this is an in-depth (and much-needed!) representation of US electoral politics that I'll be playing again and again. (How odd that nothing's ever filled the niche all that well until now, despite the gaminess built into the Constitution.) I can definitely see explaining this to a fairly handy gamer and playing twice in an evening.

Also, Z-Man deserves a lot of credit for putting together such gorgeous parts. The state seals are perfect, the board is legible, and the cards are substantial and well-designed, with excellent photos. No more "Chili!"

[After 4 plays: this game is fantastic. There are some warts, and they're not necessarily easy to iron out, but it's still a superb game.]

[After 9 plays: Now that I've been through this a few times, I spend most of every game sitting in New York and Pennsylvania, with occasional forays to California, Texas and Ohio. It's a bit of a problem with the design... and American politics, really.]

RATING: 7
Nicely dense card mechanics - each card can either be played to manipulate the gamestate, banked for its corner value to spend in later phases, or discarded to generate action points (with your opponent being given the option to pay to trigger its text effect anyway). Good gameplay and a nice history lesson, but the endgame feels unsatisfying, with the victor being unclear until all the fiddly counting-up is done.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
CRITIQUE OF 1960
I bought 1960 because I had been playing a lot of Labyrinth and Twilight Struggle, and I wanted more of the same. But while 1960 shares a designer with TS, the two games are very different.

In TS, the tension not only comes from having to play cards that are good for your opponent (trying to minimize the damage you do to yourself is a huge part of the game), but also from having to choose between using a card for its ops or for its event. And why that's a tough call is because the difference between the two often comes down to a single point one way or the other.

But when I played 1960, I felt none of that tension. The events on the cards were always significantly better than using the card for ops, so the game came down to who got the better cards at the right time. 1960 isn't nearly as strategic as TS, and failing to be deeply strategic it also fails at replicating the tension of that famous presidential election.

I love the components; the graphic design of the game is solid and everything is well made. Also, the theme and the history lesson is very well done! People these days would probably be shocked to learn that the southern states were mostly "blue" when people voted their social and economic interests (i.e., before the Republicans flipped these states by appealing to Southerners' historical racist and religious fundamentalist leanings).

But honestly, after a few plays I couldn't bring myself to keep this game on my shelf. I bought it because I loved the decision-making process in card driven games like Labyrinth and Twilight Struggle, and that process is simply lacking here. I've seen 1960 referred to as "Twilight Struggle Lite," but it's not. Very little of what makes TS so satisfying is present in this game.

RATING: 4.5
[2008.Mar.09] A visually stunning design produced by Z-Man Games, however, after a few plays the game appears to lack the staying power of its predecessor, Twilight Struggle (TS). With only one deck (as opposed to separated decks, like in TS) and no opportunity to re-shuffle (and thus, re-draw/re-encounter cards), the event cards are a little random in their appearance and one or two turns of 'bad' card draws may easily cost you the game. Additionally, the game eventually devolves into the same arm-wrestling contest centered around a handful of large (electoral college-wise) States. ("I put cubes there, and then you take them off; rinse and repeat.") In the end, 1960 removes some of the elements that I didn't like in TS, but unfortunately also removes many the elements that I did like.

:star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:

RATING: 6
I prefer Twilight Struggle over 1960 but if you are looking to play a similar styled game in less than half the time, this is your game.  After my first play, I'm not certain how much value the endorsements provide, but that may just be because of our style of play the first time.  Using momentum to allow you to either use your own events on cards played by others is an interesting twist as is the cubes in the bag for drawing for influence in states where the other person has 4 cubes or their candidate.  The player aid on the back page of the instructions is well done and the instructions are laid out clearly and concisely.  I would have preferred that more cubes were included as I ran out of cubes quite often in the later rounds, but this appears to generally only happen when one person is playing poorly or has had considerable misfortune.  Its a fun game, but I do question the replayability factor of this game compared to the more extensive Twilight Struggle which feels much more or a life and death struggle.  This game seems to quickly devolve into 1-3 battleground states where most of the attention gets focused thus far.

After more plays of Twilight Struggle, I've concluded that I would just prefer to play TS over 1960.  1960 is a fine game that may be more user friendly, but it just doesn't carry the same delightful tension of card to card play that TS provides.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
This truly belongs to the best political games out there and represents a fascinating exercise in American politics. Another wonderful implementation of the card driven mechanic (see, e.g., Hannibal: Rome vs Carthage or Twilight Struggle). It manages to create the atmosphere and tension of an ongoing campaign (never run a campaign myself, of course, but it does meet my idea of how it could be like).
Everything seems to fall into its place, with tough decisions on whether to invest influence into media, into campaigning states or into the candidates profile regarding three issues (Civil Rights, Defense, and Finance). Although I really like 1960, I have to confess that, for some reason I cannot pinpoint, I would most of the times prefer Twilight Struggle.

RATING: 8
A topic I always want to know more. 1960: The Making of the President...after Obama was in office for 2 years, I finally got a chance to play this game which was bought about when Obama was sworn into office. Easy to set up and gameplay flows smoothly with a simpler mechanics than Twilight Struggle, if only it was because I was familiar with the designer's intent. The story rolled out quite naturally with the cards and the text and the game has a stronger story-telling ability. The game balance was very close, more than I expected initially. The game was won only in the last turn when attacks made at New York could have swung the whole thing from the Democrats to the Republicans. However, I still felt the game was slightly in favor of Kennedy. Maybe that advantage would be dissipated after more plays. Final game result was Kennedy 312 vs. Nixon 225. Both NY and PA are critical states and I suspect they have to be taken in any game in order to claim a victory. So despite definitely 1960: The Making of the President is a good game, I am not sure if the game can be somehow different and if other lesser states can make the difference. It might be true to history and reality. In game terms, it might also mean restricted variability for repeated play. I need more plays other than anything else to confirm such observation though.

It s a very well designed, ver smooth election game. The cards are meaningful and impactful. All factors affecting an election campaign are genuinely identified and integrated with the three major national issues. An admirable game.

RATING: 7
Simple enough to allow players to make difficult and meaningful decisions on their first play.  Deep enough to be enjoyed more and more beyond the first play (as one gets a better knowledge of the interplay between the different mechanisms, and of what cards are found in the deck).  However, the game beginning is not as tense as the beginning in Schotten-Totten, or Samurai: there are many possibilities open (the situation becomes tighter towards the end).

The theme is very well reflected in the mechanics.  This makes the game more enjoyable (for instance, the cubes you get when playing certain Events do make sense), and also gives a lesson in history (and an appreciation of how difficult campaigning is).

Very good quality bits: very thick cardboard, printing nicely centered, sturdy cards.

RATING: 2
Two-thirds of one play. 

Initially the game reminded me of reading a high school history book. In some ways this is good as the cards have a lot of flavor text, but mostly this is bad as high school history books are the best cure for insomnia that I know of.

Fast forward to the present and the game is afoot. Nixon (me) is losing. Not badly, but losing nonetheless. He decides to capture the East, specifically New York which appears to be the decider in many of the session reports on BGG. To do this, he is crafty. He gains and loses media control over a few turns until there are two media support in the East, which makes it more difficult to remove Nixon's support. Nixon continues by campaigning heavily in New York, carrying the state with 5 state support. Finally he camps out there. Any ONE of these things should make life difficult for Kennedy as support checks are now required to campaign in NY. Or so it was thought.

Two Kennedy events later, state support is destroyed without a single support check. Then something happened that has never happened in a presidential race - Nixon quit. Yep, a first for me but the game made me so mad that I quit. If all the work of many turns could be wiped out with two cards, I don't feel the game is worth playing.

In short, I knew the events were powerful, but I did not realize how devastating a good hand of events is versus a bad hand. Campaigning was a complete waste of time compared to good events. So I am not impressed.

On a positive note, this game has one of the best boards I have ever seen!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The game was pretty fun---I was worried about the  conflict, but it turned out we were okay with it.  When this type of tug-of-war conflict is [i]expected[/i] in the game, it doesn't feel as nasty and mean as when it appears more "optional".  The hand management and cardplay aspect was right up our alley.  Another reason the game didn't feel quite as nasty is that you always have something you can do with your cards, even if you have a fairly bad hand---you can play them as events or play them for points.  You then have to decide whether to play the points as campaign actions in states, on the issues, and on the media.  In fact, there's a huge amount of angst over how you should play your cards, which is the kind of tension we like.   You never feel locked out, which makes it a little more newbie friendly than [GAMEID=12333], where sometimes you're forced to play unfavorable events.  The way all of the aspects interlock together as checks and balances works fairly well (although we still haven't really gotten a good handle on preparing for the debates).

Still, the game was a success and we enjoyed it. It feels like a good introduction to card driven games, and she was even won over the election theme and learning some neat historical facts. The game length is right around how long we like it (~90 min), so it may be a while before we move into the significantly longer Twilight Struggle neighborhood, but I'm hopeful...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
You are either Nixon or Kennedy in the 1960 Race for President.  You play cards that represent real events from the campaign and can be used for "campaign points" or as events.  Opponents can use the points to impact areas of the board to increase majorities in a state, affect the media or fight for control of issues.  Opposing players can use momentum to trigger events.  

A card each round is left for the debates.  Debates are fought over issues.  

In the end, the winner is determined by summing the electoral votes for each state like a real election.  

The game is brilliant, but in the end, much of the play centers on the fight over the issues.  I suppose that makes thematic sense, but the game can be "samey" after repeated plays.

RATING: 7
A good, very playable simulation of a US election.

Feels realistic and there's lots of decisions.

However, compared to Twilight Struggle, this feels much more Euro, and is a little disappointing beauase of it.  The event cards lack variety and interest - they mostly just add or subtract cubes. Control swings gently back and forth, without the exciting combos and changes of fortune in TS.

Also, the cube bag idea, though a stunningly clever concept, is poorly implemented here and doesn't really work in this game.  The bag just gets fuller and fuller as too few things cause you to draw from it.  An odd flaw that must have been noticed in playtesting. I feel there are accolades aplenty awaiting the first person to incorporate this mechanism successfully into a game design.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This is a nicely presented game. I find American presidential politics very interesting (and I wrote that part of this comment *before* the 2016 election campaign).  The clash between Kennedy and Nixon is something that I knew of, but did not know much about.  This gave me an opportunity to learn a little more, in the context of a game.

Interestingly, the Australian Federal election in 1960 was perhaps even more of a cliffhanger than the US Presidential race - the conservatives are said to have been returned to government on the back of Communist Party preferences in the seat of Moreton - the conservative Prime Minister (Robert Menzies) having unsuccessfully attempted to ban the Communist Party five years earlier.  But that might be a topic for another day.

Anyway, nothing really to dislike about this game:  I like card driven games; I love politics and political history; the game looks and feels good.  (I am particularly fond of the mock coffee stains on the board).

I think the thing that brings me to rate it fairly low is that I prefer games with more direct conflict.  This has lots if indirect conflict.  What I am doing as I play is responding to moves my opponent has made, or making moves that force her or him to respond - setting up leads that need to be pulled back etc.  But we do not take each other on directly either in my turn or hers / his.

Undoubtedly that says more about me than it does about the game - but hence the low rating.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
1960 was THE game to get in the latter half of 2007. Luckilly I got one for Christmas and I've had the opportunity to play it about 4 or 5 times now. 

Thematically the game does a fantastic job of immersing you into the politics of 1960. The components are all top notch and they contribute greatly to the feeling of running a major political campaign.

The rules are very simple and 1960 has one of the best rulebooks out there too. There are very few ambiguities and it has plenty of diagrams to make things as clear as possible. 

From a gameplay standpoint, it succeeds on nearly all levels. Think of it as a "Variable Action Point" game with an "Area Control" board. You never have enough resources or time to do what you'd like and there is a constant struggle to defend against what your opponent is doing verses attacking him when you have an opportunity. I'm still not certain that the concerns of "Issue Wars" isn't valid as most of my games have had some of that in them. I'll probably try the two simple fixes for Issues (may not place a cube on the issue that is depicted on the card you play) and Media (May arrange issues BEFORE awards), as the concerns of Issues being too powerful and Media being too weak ring true to me.

All in all, this is a fantastic game that I just want to keep playing. After 4 games or so, I've yet to win too.

I can easily say that this game provided more tension than any other game I've played, from start to finish. 

My favorite part about the game, and the driving force throughout, is the cards. They are fantastic. First of all, the names of many of them are hilarious (political jokes at the expense of either Kennedy or Nixon). They also include just a blurb of flavor text, which in this case is historical information (which is primarily anecdotal and interesting). Beyond that, the cards are mostly split into 2 parts. You can either initiate an event OR take action points. This decision alone can drive any player mad, at times. Plus, you have to consider whether you are going to prevent someone from blocking your event or if you want to copy someone else's. Perhaps most importantly, you have to play the cards in the right order to achieve the optimal effect. To be fair, this could potentially be an AP-inducing nightmare for some player. Even for me, someone who doesn't generally succumb to AP, I tend to have to take my time. But, it's so enjoyable, and this is where the game gets a lot of its tension! It's almost more fun to decide the order to play your cards than it is to actually play them, oddly enough. So, the cards are awesome. 

Beyond that, there's a great deal of chrome in the game: endorsements, advertising, major issues, momentum, etc. Consequently, there's a lot to think about, but it's not overwhelming, at least not for us. This isn't a gateway game! If you play this as a gateway, you might hate it, or think it feels like work. But that's just an assumption, I can't be sure.

Nevertheless, one of the greatest strengths of this game is the theme. I love how everything blends thematically: the tension, the back and forth, the debates, the campaign factors, and eventually, election day. The theme is well-implemented (granted I'm a history teacher). 

The components are also quite nice. The art is period-appropoiate and clean. I like the "rest cubes/coffee mug" mechanic. What's interesting, and also thematically appropriate to elections, is I was crushing my wife the whole game as Kennedy. But because of her disproportionate amount of support check cubes in the bag on election day, you was able to pull off an inconceivable victory. I couldn't believe I lost, and I was quite frustrated, but in a weird way (because it made so much sense thematically) I just couldn't help but like the game more because of it. I just love this game! I'm going to stop now!

*with FIMO miniatures
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
2 / 5 - Fair.

Some solid strategic decisions, especially around creative ordering of card play to maximise benefits. The cube-drawing mechanics is a really nice way of getting some random elements into the game while allowing players some strategic input into tilting the odds. However, and slightly bizarrely for such a relatively simple game, there's a bit too much pointless fluff (endorsements, advertising and other mechanics don't do an awful lot). It also lacks a little in the tension department - it's there at the end, but not through the rest of the game. The fact that most cards just shuffle cubes around makes them hard to differentiate and thus makes it more difficult to relate the game to its historical roots.

RATING: 7
I liked 1960: The Making of the President. Though like most card based games the luck of the draw is huge. I think its a well designed game; the elements all seem logical, historic, and realistic. Yet there are so many facets to consider its easy for a new player to be overwhelmed by options, and how the options relate to each other. Also, like many card games, knowledge of the card deck is very helpful. Therefore I can't see a newbie winning unless he just happens to draw exceptional cards. That doesn't make 1960 a bad game, but a prospective player should be aware of this.  

As to the graphics, they are generally very good. Though there should be some display to show which player currently has the initiative. Plus printing the state names in black on a blue strip across the top of some of the state's cube boxes was a poor choice. White on blue would have been *much* easier to read. All the photos on the cards seemed period except the Recount card's photo that is clearly from the 2000 election snafu in Florida (hanging chads). 

The stated play time of 90 minutes is ridiculous. I can't see even very experienced players finishing a game anywhere close to this. My first game on 10/27/12 took 2.8 hours.
04/20/13 Played a second game in 2.6 hours. 
10/12/13 Played a third game in 2.75 hours.

RATING: 7
As with every Z-Man game, I'm really impressed by the quality of what you get. I love their boxes (though Imperial still has my favourite cover) because they're made of thick card and well-printed. The boards are some of the toughest and best-printed I've ever seen, and I really love the semi-matt finish on the maps which is classy. Though I was - and am - disappointed by Pandemic (I still can't understand what people love about this game, but each to their own. Maybe I just need competition), I think Traders of Carthage is great. Hence I wanted to try, and now I've bought this. Money well spent, even if the bits were a little mouldy. They soon cleaned up. Where the hell are Z-Man warehousing their games, a dungeon? 

Anyway, this is by far the best 2-player game I've played. At least, it suits my preference and style of play. That's not to say I like it because I win all the time - assuredly, I don't. But I think the reciprocating mechanic in card play - gain campaign points whilst risking your opponent activating an event you don't want, or activate a favourable event which might mean you take a hit somewhere else - makes for some tense and thrilling guessing games. And the fact that you're only playing one other person I find more exciting than, dare I say it, the multiplayer action in Imperial and many of the other games I own. In some ways, the chaos and unpredictability of multiplayer games means you don't feel so bad when you lose. If I'd only played the Eisenhower card there, though... and if I'd only kept a Civil Rights in my campaign stack... and one more momentum chip...

A future classic, and already one I know I'll play again and again. I guess Duel in the Dark is in my sights now.

Gone down a bit after a few plays.

RATING: 9
Brilliant game.  Awesome game design.  Great educational value.  Loads of fun, tension and excitement.

A little tough to wade through all the rules with an impatient-to-get-started crowd, but extremely satisfying once in gear.  One of the best rulebooks ever written makes it easier, but still, there's just a lot of rules to explain.  Of course, it's the dimensional aspect of each game component that is somewhat complex, but ultimately it's all very logical once understood.

I actually delayed a complete explanation of the debate and election day mechanics until a couple of turns in (only giving top level guidance for what kind of card might work best in the debates).  Once they could experience how the cards basically played, it all started to click together for them.

Played with small teams for the first time, and it played very well (although taking much longer, of course. ~3 hours with rules explanation.)

Play is richer for an older crowd (due to familiarity to the current events of the time) but still very enjoyable for smart teens.

This one was a good stretch for my new gaming crew, and probably garnered the most lively after-game conversations.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Game in a Nutshell: Card driven competition over election year 1960, where several components (media, debates, campaigning, etc.) all play in tipping the scales for each state's electoral votes.

Played as: Two-player only.

Pros: Absolutely every turn is as important as the last. There will be no winning this game in the first 30 minutes of play, and chances are when it's all said and done, there's going to be a race that's so tight, it'll likely be decided by a few well placed cubes. The closeness of each contest of course is because of some nice built in mechanics that only allow the smallest of advantages to maybe slip through from round to round.

Cons: There is a small random factor that can make a big difference on election day when drawing cubes from the bag. The theme was an initial turnoff.

Other: This has that great combination of opponent interaction vs. hand management that I love so much in my favorite games. However, I'm worried that this game isn't quite as dynamic as others and will wear out faster due to a more structured progression of play. I was pleasantly surprised that the game, labeled with such a boring title, actually has very tight and exciting game-play.

Best Use: Right now it's the best two-player gamers game I've played.

They can campaign in states, work the issues for momentum and endorsements or spend their political capital in the media. LPJ
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a great 2 player game, but you have to be able to find somebody who will enjoy it.  This game is on the long side for me, but I still enjoy it.  The problem is I don't have anybody that enjoys it enough to get a good amount of plays out of it, so it mostly just sits on my shelf.

This game is all about the Kennedy/Nixon presidential race.  I am not a huge history buff, but I still think the theme is great.  The game starts with each player starting in their home state and some of the states already having some support cubes for one side or the other.  These are based on some historical data for the states that already had a huge support for one candidate or the other in the beginning.  On a typical turn each player is dealt some cards.  These cards represent events that actually happened throughout the presidential race.  The cards are marked clearly as to which candidate the card favors, so when deciding which cards to use you can quickly see if one will help you or not.  5 of these cards will get played during the round.  When you play a card you must decide if you want to use the card for the event, usually only if it helps you, or if you want to use it for the number of actions on the card.  The actions allow you to move your candidate to a new region, remove your opponents support cubes in a state, add your own support cubes to a state, add support cubes to the debate issues, or try to get some advertising going in a given region.  When a player plays a card and chooses to use the actions, the other player can force the event to happen, because a lot of times this card might help them, by playing one of their campaign tokens.  If you can predict that the player might do this, as you play the card, you have the option of playing 2 campaign tokens and saying that they can't trigger this event.  After the round awards are given, endorsements or more campaign tokens, for cubes in the debate section.

After a few rounds the debates come up.  During the rounds each player uses one of the cards to save for the debates.  In the debates you add cards for a given issue, marked on the card, and when one side has 2 cards for that issue it is then tallied up to see who wins it.  The winner has 2 actions to spend on state support for the first issue, 3 for the next and 4 for the last.

After two more normal rounds, this time you play two extra cards for additional support at the end of the game in a given state, again marked on the cards, the states are awarded to the candidates.  If a state has one of your cubes on it, you win it, if it is empty and you have endorsements in that region you win it, if it is empty and there aren't any endorsements it goes to the color as marked on the state.   The electoral votes are then tallied and the winner becomes president.

I really love this game and I give it a high rating because I would play it any chance I could get.  The theme might not be enjoyable by all.  Understanding how to get and use campaign tokens can be tricky for people the first time.  These are a few things that can turn people off to the game, not to mention it usually takes around 2 hours to play.  If you aren't distracted by any of those issues, this could be a great game for you.  It provides you with some tough decisions and really feels like you are fighting it out with the other player to see who will be the president.  Is it more important to hit the states, work on the debate issues, or get some more advertising?

I love it.

RATING: N/A
Long 2 player, area-control game that is similar to Twilight Struggle.

Each player uses cards to try to influence the 50 states. 5ish rounds are played, then debates happen, then 2 more rounds are played and then the election happens. Whoever has sufficient number of electoral votes wins.

It sounds ok to play, but I'm not into the theme and it runs long for a 2 player for me.

RATING: 9
Move over Die Macher, 1960 is nipping at your heels.  This game may not have the depth of Die Macher, but the streamlined complexity and ability to be played in less than 2 hours (rather than 5) makes it a worthy contender for best election game currently on the market.  It really captures the feel of limited resources, key states, and multiple ways to win and election   Also, the history factor is a great draw for me.  Just as in Twilight Struggle, this game is a must for any history teacher (especially Civics or Political Science).  Awards: International Gamers Award-Two Player (2007).

RATING: 6
The more I play this game, the more I wish I was playing Twilight Struggle instead.  It may be unfair to compare 1960 to its older brother all the time, but the fact is that these two games occupy very similar positions in my gaming repetoire, and playing 1960 necessarily means that I'm not playing Twilight Struggle.  1960 does have some wonderful mechanics, especially everything surrounding the cube bag.  It does a great job of introducing an element of luck while simultaneously balancing the game.  Any pain you may feel from losing initiative or getting a draw of low CP cards will be mitigated by gains in the cube bag.

Unfortunately, the cube bag isn't used enough in the game, and this is because of the power of the cards.  This is one of my complaints with 1960; the relative power of the events is so great that the game loses the tension of deciding how to use your cards.  Whereas in Twilight Struggle you must give up something when you use the card as points or vice versa, in 1960 the decision is pretty binary.  If the event helps you at all, you play the card for the event.  If it does not, you use it for points.  A side effect of this is that players have many, many opportunities to place influence in states without obeying the normal influence placing rules.  This means that the location of your candidate and having 4 or more cubes in a location isn't really very important.  And because players can easily add cubes to a state being carried by their opponent, they rarely need to make support checks from the bag.  This means that the balancing mechanic doesn't come into play that often.

There are other things missing from 1960 as well.  Any sort of board positioning is unimportant, because of the power of the cards (as I mentioned above), and because of the ease of placing influence in a location.  This is understandable considering the theme, but it still something I miss.  In addition, the large draw deck means that it is very difficult to plan for any cards other than what you have in your hand.  This isn't necessarily bad, but once you've played Twilight Struggle, you can see the strategic additions that the 3 different decks adds.  Finally, the fact that your opponent's events don't happen automatically is certainly more forgiving, but again detracts from the tension of playing your hand.  A few momentum markers and you don't even have to worry.

1960 isn't a bad game.  It's actually a good one (though I have played almost a dozen times now, and Kennedy has never won).  It's just that it isn't really anything that different from Twilight Struggle in terms of when and who I play it with, so it ends up being an inferior experience.  I understand why so many people like it, and it may in fact be better for those people, but it isn't a better game.  And frankly, I don't see myself playing it very often.

RATING: 7
Solid, solid, solid two person game that simulates the 1960 election in an entertaining and competitive manner.  The game is surprisingly quick, too.  My first play clocked in at just over an hour, had minimal rule look ups, and did not feel like a real game with tough decisions right off of the bat.  Very similar mechanics to Twilight Struggles with just a few wrinkles to make it its own game, and anyone looking to see how a game can be an educational endeavor should experience this game.

edit: After more plays, downgraded from an 8 to a 7.  Still very much enjoy the game, and really like the educational aspect.  However, it does seem that there is more-or-less a rule for how to play.  Always play about 1/3 - 1/2 of your cards to issues; use card effects to deal with 4+ control; use CPs to duke it out for the 5 or so states with the most electoral votes; and absolutely focus on that last turn.  For two players that know the game, the score is always going to be close, and is going to come down a bit to the luck of what cards you've got when.  In that sense it mirrors the actual election, but for a game, it comes off a little bit samey.  I'll always play, though, so it's a rock solid 7.

RATING: 9
Greatly enjoyed playing this game. I love the hand management and the back and forth trying to gain support in states while setting up events to help me and minimize the help to my opponent while having to struggle with long term strategy. I love the moment at the start of a new turn when you have a bunch of cards you have to struggle to deal whether it is a garbage hand or even a great hand that needs to be played just right. The rebate was a bit of a let down but I can see its value. Next time I play I will have a better I idea how much or how little I should invest into it. I have also played Twilight Struggle which has many of the same game mechanics but I believe 1960 is much more elegant game. The tug-a-war with the cubes works better than the chit-fest of TS and I much prefer pulling cubes from the bag for randomness than the simple dice roll because it helps counter balance the game with poor hands. I like the momentum tokens OK, although I miss the way that in TS opponents events are always played so you have to do a lot more damage management and the gaining momentum cards are a much weaker version of the scoring cards in TS. Even with these minor issues I still found 1960 a much more enjoyable experience than TS even though I lost my first game at 1960 and won my first game of TS.

RATING: 9
I like politics and I love political history, but I'd thought I'd hate them in a game. On the surface this struck me as a bit of a snooze but how wrong I was. This is an excellent game that cleverly combines hand management and area control. I would posit that the Nixon player seem to have a bit of an advantage (the Nixon player has won about 90% of the 20 or so games we've played) due to the geographical starting positions but that's my only gripe with this game. It is now my favourite two-player game, and it plays under the 2-hrs mark. With 1960 every day is a SuperTuesday!

RATING: 8

It leaves things tense without feeling arbitrary.  Easy bump up of one point, and I can imagine it seeing more.  Strong recommendation.

RATING: 7
After now having played this twice I thought I'd better add at least a small comment. This game really is great and I really have had fun both times I've  played it and not just fun in places fun throughout (apart from one notable exception). The components are great and well designed, the cards interesting and well balanced and the game play fast and inspiring. The only one niggle I have with this game is the debates section. Now stop right there before you lambast me for being unintelligent enough to not know how the American election works or for being presumptuous enough to think that it should be simply done away with. I can see the need for it both as a representation of reality and as a game mechanic, it's just that the mechanic seems wrong somehow; Let me explain, most players including myself use the strategic area to hide cards that they don't want there opponent to get the use of for the first six rounds (sound plan you may say to yourself). This means that when you get to the debate you just hand issues to your opponent, how does that make sense? So now you might say "well just put put cards you can use in your strategic area?" "Well." I would reply. You then give your opponent accesses to there powerful cards and as you only win a max of 4CP in the debate what the hell would be the point in that? So on the whole great game with a slightly strange bit in the middle.

RATING: 6
For the first 2 or 3 plays through, 1960: The Making Of The President gives the feel of being a political campaign manager in the form of a war game. Early plays are fun, grueling contests to win the hearts and minds of the American people and provide an education of the U.S. political landscape in 1960.

The game is as well-designed as it could be. Each game component is attractive and well-implemented. For new players, rules are not too difficult to learn. A curious addition to the game is the debate turn which uses mechanics similar to Schotten-Totten to win debate issues.

1960:tMotP initially only suffers from the fact that it is for 2-players only, but after a few plays its other limitations will appear. Once a player becomes familiar with, or memorizes, the cards the game loses its appeal. Variability dissolves as players move toward set strategies making each playthrough a forgone conclusion. Experienced players will unavoidably slaughter beginners.

All that was needed to improve 1960:tMotP was too have more cards than could be used in the course of the game. This would have added some uncertainty to each play mixing things up for veterans.

Another minor quibble: The cards seem to have a bias towards the Republicans. This may be thematic, but can be problematic when introducing 1960:tMotP to new players as most people I've played it with prefer to play Democrats and fight an uphill battle all game.

RATING: 8
Jason Matthews' follow-up to the blockbuster Twilight Struggle applies similar mechanics to the 1960 elections in the US - cards as driving force by means of managing small hands, territorial control of different regions and some extra features to get rid of the least useful cards. 

Even though the topic isn't as prone to a linear approach and thus doesn't grab as quickly, 1960 does improve some of the flaws that ever so slightly pop up in Twilight Struggle: the only randomizer are the card draws each turn, there aren't surprise scoring rounds during the game, the distribution of forces on the board can be influenced by some "regional parameters" (media and endorsement) and there are two fixed phases (debates and election) with separate procedures to use discarded cards tactically as well.

1960 might take more effort to digest than Twilight Struggle and offer less immediately compelling gameplay, but there are more layers in strategy and tactics; plus, skillful moves never get marred by a fatal dice throw.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Good game to play in an election year. And it's a game that everyone should try at least once. It's a great mix of euro mechanics and history. This shows that games can be both fun AND educational. I were teaching a US history or a civics class, I would include this game in my curriculum.

It's a good game to play, despite some imbalances with the mechanics; some are anemic and never get used (media), others are over-powered (issues) - which might be okay if it were closer to reality. Overall, I think the mechanics of Twilight Struggle don't translate as well to elections as they do to Cold War issues.

RATING: 7.5
規則:
*州徽正面放到版圖對應州區，根據每個州格右下符號放對應數目的顏色方塊，放候選人標記到家鄉州，事件板塊放在圖版右邊對應事件格上，回合跟階段軌第一格放上白色方塊，在袋中放兩色各10個方塊，競選卡洗勻蓋著放一旁，支援牌洗勻蓋著跟支援標記放一旁(方塊，支援標記跟陣營標記不夠用找其他東西取代)
*每個人選一個陣營拿該陣營方塊，對應候選人卡跟兩個對應陣營標記
*遊戲進行9回合，除了6跟9以外每回合都是四個階段
1.主動階段 發競選卡，前五回合六張，最後兩回合7張，不夠發棄牌洗勻在發，從袋中抽決定主動權，先抽到第二顆同色的玩家獲得，超出了返還該玩家，該玩家選擇誰先
2.活動階段 從起始玩家開始輪流出牌直到出完五張，選擇如下，如果是競選牌會放牌左側數目方塊到圖板你顏色的休息區
>使用卡牌下方事件，處理完移除，永久效果放到對應玩家前面前事件區，回合結束移除，永久事件放到對應影響區
*失去方塊最多移除到沒有，不會放其他色
>使用卡牌左方行動點，每個行動點行動可以做以下行動三選一，不能同時
>>>放方塊  放方塊到你的候選人標記所在位置，如果有其他顏色方塊則要先移除，放之前可移動候選人標記，同大區不花點數，到不同區如果跨界要花一點(阿拉斯加跟夏威夷移動時算跨界)，放的地點如果有對方候選人或是對方方塊達到四個且沒有自己媒體控制(該區有一個方塊就算)時要做支持確定，從袋中抽想要放數量的方塊，幾個自己顏色才能放幾個，抽出來的都放到各自擁有者的供應區，如果袋子空了則在補充兩色各10個
>>>控制媒體 抽袋中抽對應行動點的方塊，自己顏色的就放到任意媒體區(有其他色改為移除)，其他顏色還給對應玩家
>>>控制議題 放置方塊在三個議題上，有其他色改移除，每個議題同回合第一個花1點，第二個以後花兩點
*如果對方使用完行動點，你可以花一個競選標記來啟動該卡事件並移除，你不會因此放方塊到休息區，對方可花兩個競選標記來防止你啟動該事件(他出牌時就要宣告)
>使用面前的競選人卡 使用上面的競選點數選上方三個行動之一做，之後卡翻面
3.動力階段 棄掉一半的競選人標記(向下取整)，結算三個議題，第三名議題拿一個標記，第二名拿一個標記或是抽一張支持卡結算，第一名則兩者都有，之後所有議題上方移除一個方塊，然後媒體區總和方塊多的可交換兩個相鄰議題的位置(平手就不換)
*結算支持卡時馬上放一個支持標記在對應州的媒體區，如果該區有對手的則改成移除
4.競選策略階段 前五回合蓋一張放到自己圖版顏色區，後兩回合蓋兩張，剩下的手牌棄掉，然後將休息區的方塊丟到袋中
*四張收集動力牌不能成為最後的蓋牌，如果要蓋要改成棄掉
*第六回合-辯論 有五個階段如下
1.主動階段 同之前決定主動玩家，此階段的平手視為主動玩家贏
2.競選策略 拿回版圖上五張自己蓋的競選牌
3.設定州 放置辯論圖版，將議題板塊放到競選圖板上，照順序
4.辯論 玩家同時出一張牌，根據下方議題跟陣營放到對應辯論圖版區，有兩個陣營可自己選擇放哪邊，當一個議題有一方有兩張以上卡時結算，多個同時則最右邊先，皆總兩方卡牌上點數多的贏，該議題板塊放回主圖版議題去最下方格，卡牌棄掉，第1/2/3個結算議題獲勝玩家拿2/3/4個方塊可放圖板任意州區，此階段當三個議題都結算後結束，或是五張牌出完後，照點數結算
*已結算的議題之後出的牌都棄掉
5.棄掉所有競選策略牌
*第九回合-投票 有6個階段如下
1.將所有媒體跟議題上方塊放到袋中，每一個競選人標記換兩個方塊到袋中
2.照前面決定主動玩家
3.從圖板拿回四張之前蓋的牌並打開，從袋中抽三個方塊，只放自己顏色的到該牌右下的州區(對手的還他)，主動玩家先放，卡牌結算順序自選
4.投票日事件 結算之前出的投票日事件卡，主動玩家選擇結算順序
5.結算空白州  如果該大區媒體有支持標記則放方塊到該大區的所有空白州，否則根據該州格的顏色放方塊
6.玩家拿取所有有自己顏色方塊州的州徽，加總後面數字，超過269的贏
*某些事件會導致沒到四顆方塊的區拿不到州徽，此時總和如果沒有超過269就總票數和，在平手比州徽數

RATING: 8
Less frustrating than TS and 1989, thanks to the use of the 'cubes in a bag' mechanism to resolve support checks. No 'all or nothing' die rolls. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A CDG playable in 2 hours. There's really nice decisions each turn on which cards to play for events, which for adding cubes on states (preferably the big VP earning ones), and how much you'll need to commit to the issues wars (which allow you to get the necessary momentum markers to play your opponent's events, plus the endorsement markers needed to win ties). Sometimes it descends to a bit of pushme-pullyou tit-for-tat, but the attraction of playing events eventually gets over it. There can be a dramatic finale with game-deciding fortunate / unfortunate swings on the last plays of the game which you need to be comfortable with. Other than that, a very enjoyable system with rich decision making throughout.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I seem to spend most of this game without a clue of how I am doing votes wise, but maybe I should just accept that there isn't a VP track around the edge of the board to keep me perfectly informed, and play the game on gut feeling. You could constantly count up exactly how you were doing in votes, but it would make for a mighty long game - instinctively, I go for the big number states and try to win (or at least stay in contention) in these, and if I can pick up the odd 5 or 10 vote state along the way, so much the better. This approach for me recreates the fog-of-war which the respective campaigns must have felt historically, and is probably the spirit in which the designers would want the game to be played.

There are different ways to win the game (never a bad thing), but if your opponent is concentrating on Issues, for example, you had better put some effort into issues as well, or you will find yourself behind in Endorsements very quickly.

All-in-all, a solid 9 at the minute, with lots more games planned.

RATING: 4
Pros:
+Rulebook is one of the best I've seen. It didn't quite click for me until the example of play at the end, but having that included was great. It's very well laid out with great examples.
+Map looks really nice and the artwork is well done.
+Cards are historic and as far as I know, all accurate.
+Components look and feel great. Cards are linen finished and cubes are a nice deep blue/red.
+Plays reasonably quick.

Cons:
-A little hard for my head to fully wrap around the rules, but after I got a play in and read the sample of play, it all seemed so easy.
-Very math heavy at the end adding up the electoral votes.
-Seemed to be short on cubes the first play, will change this on subsequent plays if that's not the case.
-Could see this getting stale after a bit, but I really can't wait to give this a lot more of my time.

In addition, I have played Twilight Struggle and now feel foolish for ever thinking this could touch it. At least these guys know how to make me care about history.

RATING: 4
A bit of a disappointment, really. TS is one of my all time favorites and the prospect of having a similar game playable in 1.5 hours seemed great. This is not that game, though.

It's playable in 90 minutes, give or take a few. I think luck plays a very significant role, much bigger than in TS, even though there are no dice. The problem is that the luck of the draw can be decisive and the game doesn't provide the players with the tools to deal with it. Rest cubes seem to be the way to balance things out but I don't think they're powerful enough. Campaigning and positioning on the issues are very zero-sum actions, so the whole game becomes a boring and repetitive exercise.

The components are great, the theme is nicely connected to some of the game mechanics and everything is balanced.

RATING: 7
Though this isn't Twilight Struggle, I enjoy the theme of this game, as well as the relatively simpler way it captures the decisions of heavier CDGs. The three major shortcomings for me are the fairly arithmetic-heavy scoring, which makes it tedious to assess how one is doing midgame, the much lower tension of holding your opponent's events (relative to TS) given the relative scarcity of momentum markers, and the fact that it's so easy to undo the opponent's influence that the game often involves a large portion of back-and-forth over the big states.

RATING: 9
A brilliant meshing of theme and mechanisms. Every move seems to hold the promise of furthering the cause; the push and pull of regional swings, resource management, timing your moves just so, whether to put efforts into the Media now or wait a few turns, when to press for leadership on issues...the full swing of a presidential race pulls you in and keeps you guessing until the final turn on Election Day. 

If there is a weak point to the game, like others have stated, I would cite the round of Debates; it's not that it doesn't work...it does, and it's interesting as a break from the other turns surrounding it...but it feels anti-climactic and doesn't always yield as much advantage as a few well-timed cards might also bring. This is a truly minor quibble, however; the flow of the game is always exciting, always challenging.

RATING: 6
A game I’d been wanting to try for ages, having adored Twilight Struggle and enjoyed Campaign Manager. I had always assumed it would have much more in common with TS, but in fact the rapid tit-for-tat exchanges feel more like CM. Unfortunately, I think it may fall between two stools. It’s not as quick and tactical as CM but nor does it have the deep strategic tension and satisfaction of TS. The main problem is that, unlike TS, it lacks a real spatial dimension, and mostly lacks a time dimension too. Playing cubes into a state early doesn’t really give any benefit over playing them late, especially as carrying a state doesn’t feel as strong as control in TS. And there’s no in-game scoring (fair enough given the theme) so it’s all about getting the last say. This puts more focus on the rewards you can get during the game, leading to a tit-for-tat battle over the issues. The other big difference over TS is that I just don’t feel scared looking at a hand full of opponent events. The momentum system means you can usually preempt one, ditch one, and play any others when they don’t do much damage. And it usually seems obvious when to play a card as event or for campaign points - particularly as many of the events are strictly better in the number of campaign point equivalents they give you. On the plus side, it is half the length of TS and easier to get into, but as the streamlined Age of Industry is to the clunky masterpiece Brass, so 1960 is to the miraculous TS.

RATING: 8
Is 1960 as good as it spiritual ancestor - Twilight Struggle? The short answer is not quite. I still prefer Twilight Struggle

The long answer:
Matthews kept the core mechanic of events for your opponent being triggered when you play the card. It has been tweaked in that the opponent must use a momentum marker to trigger the event. 
Is the game thematic? That is the mechanics are in line with the theme.
The core play is to use your action points to jockey for positions in the various states. Which like the campaigns focuses on the key states for the electoral vote.
Playing to issues is how you get momentum markers and endorsements. So the issues are important. The markers allow you steal the events from your opponent's play and many of the events are dependent on controlling an issue. 
The endorsements make it easier to control a region since you only have to tie (and not beat your opponent).
Finally the media allows for effective counter-attacks to help you reclaim lost ground.

The games looks to be more balanced than Twilight Struggle - don't have the asymmetry forced by the divided decks. However I enjoyed the intermediate regional scoring of TS more. However, the final election is the theme of 1960.

All in all a tense game with an evocative theme.

RATING: 8.5
It's complex without being complicated (takes some effort to learn the rules yet play is pretty second nature once you get going), and enjoyable enough for normal gamers while also stroking my poli-sci nerdity. I've played several election board games and this one is the only one to make campaigning not-so-clunky.

As somebody leery of GMT products because of the assumption they're overly rule-heavy and serious I'm both happy to have bought and played the Z-man version to give it a chance before the reboot and also happy that GMT picked it up to keep it in print because it's a worthy game. Aesthetically, however, I prefer the Z-man original.

RATING: 9.5
Végre! Kipróbáltuk ezt a remek játékot. Sajnos megint beleszaladtam abba, hogy csak nekem tetszett. Ez a jövőt illetően nem sok jóval kecsegtet. 
Rövid véleményem az, hogy egy jó kis mechanikával és remekül átérezhető tematikával van felvértezve. Nagyon jó minőségű a játék, kifejezetten a kártyák. Ezek messzemenőkig a legszebbek amivel valaha játszottam, sőt a minősége is remek. Komoly mélységű stratégiákat nem tudtunk még játszani, mert homály volt ezzel kapcsolatban mindegyikünkbe, de a fő csapásirányokat már megláttuk. De a tématámogatások a végén erősek lehetnek, csak a párom ezt szerintem nem használta ki százszázalékosan. A vita szerepét nem látom olyan kiemelkedőnek, de ahhoz többet kellene játszani, hogy ezt bátran kijelentsem. Jó sok minden összefügg a kártyákkal kapcsolatban, de mégsem vagyunk szerintem urai a játékunknak, egy kicsit a szerencse is közbeszólhat. Bár a fene tudja, olyan nagyon mégsem ül rá a játékra ez a szerencse. Kíváncsi lennék ezeknek a kocka bedobás, kihúzás matematikai összefüggéseire.
Summa summarum ez egy jó játék. Üröm az örömben, hogy egy államjelzőt nem találok. (Meglett):)

Még annyit, hogy nem írtunk történelmet, csak ismételtük. Kennedy nagyon nyert!
Update: még mindig izgi, mily meglepő.:) New yorkot elvesztettem kennedyként,(illetve az utolsó pillanatban nem sikerült fordítanom ott, így full bukó lett a játékom. De izgi volt nagyon!!!

RATING: 8
Really good, tense game ala Twilight Struggle.  Fabulous job by Zman in its production.  Look and be ashamed, GMT.  The debates are a brilliant new addition, and occur just at the right point in the game.    A couple smallish things keep it from being 10-worthy in my book:  I think the "issues war" can be a problem -- just not a very compelling game play to go tit-for-tat, though I think that is the smart thing to do sometimes.  I think one of the variants posted here might be a good fix to this, though I am anxious to hear from more play testers.  Also, I think it would be useful to know the score during the game.  It makes sense that there is only one scoring round, given the theme and all, but I kind of miss the sense of momentum and narrative that comes from knowing how you are doing during the play.  There is a nice on-line scoreboard posted here in the files section that we used and liked during the last play.   mlg 1/16/08.

RATING: 8.4

Issues: I sway back and forth on this mechanic.  As I play it more, it seems too powerful.  Ignoring it is not an option, but if 2 experienced players spend all game trying to grab an advantage, its boring.  There is a balancing factor in the quality of event you sacrifice, but for many cards (and virtually all 2CP cards) its worth it to play to issues.  that seems a bit too luck based.

On the whole a highly enjoyable game.

RATING: 10
Good production quality. It's a very good card-driven game: thematic, with multi-purpose cards, balanced, with good tactical depth and some relevant strategic considerations. The game uses the U.S. map as the board where red (Republicans) and blue (Democrat) cubes are placed to mark control of each State. It is played in 9 rounds, one of which is the televised debate, and another - the last one - is the climatic and tense election day. Also, the designers couldn't find a better election to depict than the Presidential one from 1960: a very close dispute between two men that would become key figures of the 20th century - Kennedy and Nixon.

RATING: 7
More enjoyable than Twilight Struggle at first sight and the gameplay flows a bit smoother and nicer. Less stressful and a bit more fun to play than TS. The theme and the mechanisms integrade a bit better in here. Yet, I strongly think that you'll find much deeper and richer game in Twilight Struggle(or in other heavier CDGs) after a few plays, if that's something you are looking for. The swingy nature of the different events was quite laughable sometimes but yet the game came down to a single cube draw and the scoring was tight after all. I think it's in balance, just that the events were quite off-putting at times and actions were nullfied constantly by both sides. The randomness of the cube draw seemed quite high at times. The incentive for the big cities seems to be quite strong, although different events may direct you elsewhere, in any case quite straightforward gameplay on the map. I would rate it as a 8 for the enjoyment but I can't help to drop it to a 7 due the swingy nature of the events and the randomness of the cube draw.

RATING: 6.5
This is a fun game more than a serious one. You race around throwing down cards and gathering support. In technical terms its just an area control game with historical chrome and because there are only 16 random events which being drawn from the same once used deck are effectively independent there is a lot of luck in it.
However, the important thing is it looks and feels like an election campaign and with the flavour on the cards it is entertaining. A better game than some of the other card driven games though not as good a simulation as many.

Obwohl das Sujet aus heutiger Sicht vielleicht nicht zu spannend ist, können doch die unzweideutigen Regeln, der abwechslungsreiche, schnelle Rundenverlauf und die Tatsache überzeugen, daß hier a.) ein scheinbar schon enteilter Konkurrent noch lange problemlos eingeholt werden kann und b.) das abschließende Wahlergebnis durchaus größere und kleinere Überraschungen bereit hält. Außerdem sticht ins Auge, daß die damals aktuellen Politikfelder und die damaligen ideologischen Positionen der Demokraten und Republikaner sich von den heutigen wenig nur unterscheiden.
