RATING: 7
(+) Beautiful game!

(+) Simple yet interesting mechanic in which the order in which to carry out sequential card actions and careful management of momentum markers can have devestating effects on you or your opponent.

(+) Playing the democrats or the republicans really feels different. In our games, both parties have however snatched away decicive victories, so the game feels balanced (however: new players might find Nixon slighty easier or more 'straight forward', but don't be fooled, Kennedy is also a very strong faction!).

(+) The game kind of tells a story, and it is so much fun getting caught up in it! The game really makes you feel like you are running in an election.

(-) Pretty harsh game. When some cards fall on the wrong time, this could very much screw you. This kind of luck dependence is also present during cube draws from the bag. However, it is certainly possible to mitigate your luck by making the right decisions in the course of the game.

(-) This game is very 'in your face'. You directly oppose one player and you do so by making him/her hurt: you constantly destoy each others carefully built support on the map and you play agressive cards which can sometimes be more confrontational than some war games I know of. It could bring back some youth traumas of building the most magnificent sand castle on the coast, just to see it destoyed by a stupid fat kid - but this time the fat kid grew up, is red or blue and it doesn't happen to you once, but about 25 times in one evening! :)

(-) It takes a rather long time to play this game, certainly for a 2-player game. This sure is an evening-filler with a playtime of 2 to 3 hours! Presumably, when you get to know the cards, the game will flow more quickly, and who knows, maybe one day we will play it in 90 - 120 minutes?

RATING: 10
Brilliant game. Gorgeous production. Plays similar to TS in that the challenge is managing the damage of your opponents events; however, 1960 provides some alternate means of overcoming the worst by forcing or blocking events with momentum markers. The trick is knowing when to expend your limited momentum markers to thwart disaster. Furthermore, 1960 does not have the constant tension of TS as the victory point track with the possibility of an early win is gone. A winner is not determined until the last turn during election day. Theme integration is as good as it gets. I can't wait to play this with my kids when they begin studying politics.

RATING: 6
Repeated plays continue to reinforce everything that I expected was in the game.  A close battle back and forth with swings in key states being the key.   A game of balancing needs in a variety of areas (states, issues, media) while trying to mitigate the actions/events of your opponent.  As one goes through the entire deck - it is not a game of bad event avoidance, but event mitigation - how to set up to accept your opponent's events to minimize their impact while maximizing yours.

RATING: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Very good, but could be much better. 
Everyone involved with this game can be very proud - it's got a real board for one! I'm such a fan of the theme and they've done a pretty good job of making a fun game that's challenging and plays quickly to boot. A bit like El Grande for two in the USA - kind of, only much better than that sounds.

I don't think there's a better mechanism than the card driven one for adding theme to a boardgame.
The cards that add historical flavour have actions that are too uninspiringly similar. I'd prefer some objectives that'll reward a candidate if they travel to a specific state and take support for that state before a certain time or event. Objectives that the player can weigh the pros and cons for and decide whether they're worth the effort or risk. Currently the objectives are similar for each player, to do with leading an issue, or not leading an issue. The cards, as they stand, unfortunately just have too much mundane cube shuffling around.

***Confusing rule regarding when you perform support checks - State support NO, campaign points or the event gives you CP to use then YES. I personally think the game would be easier and better if support checks were required for ALL event cards and debate victories when used in a carried or candidate occupied state - media control would become more meaningful in the game.***

---
New GMT version with rules changes interests me enough to pick this one up and give it another go with the new rules. I'm especially interested in the optional rule for issues.

RATING: 10
High degree of strategy, easy to teach and fast-paced, this CDG is a true peer of TS  (with major scoring/win condition changes) and that has its pros while the more luckless elements mitigate the cons - instead of nail-biting die-rolls, you control the political capital bag. Outstanding historical board and bits - even causal gamers will want to watch you play it as they're most likely voters and certainly familiar with 'the game' of a campaign as well as the map board. Wonderful concentration of gaming in the 1-2 hrs it takes especially if the gamers are too limited time-wise for anything longer or are semi-casual gamers / in the mood for something lighter.  The entire play is quite thematic and it seems that all the minor-choices scream for attention constantly.  Strategy also morphs each game I play and I pretty much only play with one other experienced player unfortunately.
I never find myself setting its status as 'Want to Play' but it seems to find table-time rather... obsessively.

RATING: 6
Simple, straightforward yet epic game where you just try to do the best with the cards you're given, and it has a great theme to boot.

Basically just try to play your favorable cards as events if possible, and play others for Control Points, taking into account some things you want and don't want to happen through the resource of momentum. You gain momentum through Issues. Debates and Campaign Strategy add that twist to keep things interesting just when you thought things were getting repetitive. The design is simple, but makes for a very clean, satisfying game with agonizing choices.

The annoying thing is that the Debate rules are very annoying to teach to casual gamers who might have been suckered in by theme and clean gameplay otherwise. Some rule misunderstanding always seems to happen when you conduct the Debates (since they happen 1 hour into the game and there's no "taste" of them prior), then it becomes too late to undo the mistakes.

Also, Advertising seems very underpowered, which doesn't click thematically. Maybe make it so the leader in Advertising gets to switch issues before collecting momentum, not after.

UPDATE: And I'm another one of those who doesn't need to play this anymore after trying Twilight Struggle. The rules complexity is about the same, but the situations in TS are a brain-melter.

4+2
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
[10 plays] An excellent card driven political campaign game. As far as the comparison to Twilight Struggle, I'll say it is better in terms of ease of play and friendliness to newbies, not as good in terms of complexity and strength of theme, but so damn different for me that I'm glad I own both and I am resentful that some people are trying so hard to pigeonhole everyone into one camp or another. 

Each time I finish, I am already looking forward to the next game eagerly. I optimistically expect this game to do that to me time after time. The production is beautiful (the cards are almost too thick), the flow of the game is smooth, the thematic elements are strong, and there are strategic and tactical depths to explore as the hands of fate throw all kinds of crap at you.

I have lost with Nixon just once, won with him twice, and won all 3 games I have played with Kennedy.

Every time I have played with Nixon I feel like I am under the gun the whole time - used and abused - almost every powerful card against me. The way I have fought this is to try and dominate the momentum markers and do better in media and advertising. Give up on CA if necessary to fight for the all powerful NY, PA, OH area. I now consider playing Nixon to be the much bigger challenge.

Update April '14: everything I've said about this game previously still stands. Love it.

Most recent play June '17.

RATING: 7.5
Good 2-player game...even if the theme won't set the world on fire. It's twilight struggle-light minus the agony and tension of really using the cards for different purposes. The 'random' die rolls have been replaced by a cube-draw system and the game clips along at a nice rate. There are always things to do each turn but sometimes feels a little mechanical (battle for high-scoring states) and it seems like spending a card to hinder an opponent or to set yourself up for the next card play is not really worth the time and effort to do so. The debates mechanic is clever but a little bit of a let-down in execution (debates simply end up as a TS 'space-race' equivalent where people dump cards that help their opponents. The scoring at the end is also a little annoying.

The game will have its fans...those who haven't played TS or those who wan't a lighter, faster affair. It's not bad but I'd rather play TS.

RATING: 5.5

Which is somewhat depressing to someone who still tries to believe in democracy.

Either that, or the game simply doesn't quite get me. Too abstract for its theme I'd say, a bit too unpredictable, and maybe more complex than it would have to be.

RATING: 8
[2011-06-25]
Nagyon kíváncsi voltam a Twilight Struggle alapmechanizmusára épülő játékra, amelyik az 1960-as amerikai elnökválasztás témáját kapta. Személy szerint a téma engem nem tántorított el, érdekes az elektori választási rendszer a tengerentúlon. A játék kinézete szerintem teljesen rendben van, nekem tetszik, a kártyák is az adott kort idézik. Meglepett, hogy a fő mechanizmuson kívül - kártyák eseményként vagy akciópontra való felhasználása - szinte mindenben különbözik a TS-től. Ez abszolút jó pont, viszont ezek a különbözőségek szerintem általában rosszabbak, mint a nagy elődnél. Ami tetszett a fix körszám, a rövidebb játékmenet, a jobban tervezhetőség. Ami kevésbé az a szerencse szerepe. Meglepő módon ebben a játékban nincs kocka, mégis nagyobb a szerencse szerepe a TS-éhez képest. A fordulóban a kezdőjátékos eldöntése, a nagy befolyás csökkentése, a választáson a négy állam befolyása, stb. mind egy zsákból való kockahúzáson alapul véletlenszerűen. Persze a zsákban levő kockák számára van befolyásunk, de azért mégiscsak szerencse. A játék folyamán úgy éreztem nagyobb a kártyák eloszlásának szerepe is. Itt szerencsétlenebb esetben rosszabb helyzetbe kerülhet a játékos, mint a TS-ben rossz kártyák húzásakor. Érdemes a játékban tényleg mindenre figyelni, a három fő kérdéskör befolyásolása, a média vezetése és a sima kampányolás is mind-mind nagyon fontos. Külön fordulót kapó elnökjelölti vita fontosságát viszont kicsinek érzem, talán jobb az ellenfél erős lapjait ide felhasználni, mint kijátszani játék közben. Magánál a kampányolásnál is jobbnak érzem a TS-beli szomszédos befolyásolás lehetőségét. Nem rossz játék az 1960, de a TS számomra jobb, hogy mennyivel azt egy játék után nehéz megítélnem. Most egy ponttal adok csak kevesebbet neki, de elképzelhető, hogy a pontszám csökkeni fog még egyet, annál viszont semmiképp nem rosszabb, azaz aki a témát kedveli és kétszemélyes játékra vágyik annak bátran ajánlhatom. 

[2011-07-01]
Sajnos a továbbra is csak azt mondhatom nagy a szerencse szerepe, az utolsó forduló is túl sokat számít. Sokkal jobb a TS játékon belüli pontozása. Itt minden csak a végén dől el, ami nagyrészt a szerencsén múlik. Valószínűleg még 1 értéket csökkenni fog a pontszám.

[2012-02-25]

RATING: 7.5
Masterfully done election game.  Full of color, thematic integration and taught excitement.  It really has you on the edge of your seat for most of the game.  I wish I could give it a higher rating, but I just despise the debate so much that I can't do it in good conscious.  Outside of the debate being too inflexible to suit my tastes (two cards to end this is irritating with simultaneous reveals), it relies on proper card draws earlier on in the game to be effectual.  I feel that even planned for it can go sour too easily causing a huge swing in cubes.  I understand this is thematically accurate so I grant them that.  My real gripe though is that it is shunted into the heart of the game, forcing a whole different mechanic that feels completely out of place.  You have to move to a different board and play out a whole different scenario.  Like some weird mini-game.  I'd rather just concede the entire debate than to bother actually playing it.

Still the rest of the game is so brilliant, that I forgive it.

It does kind of suck that they didn't put enough cubes in the game to actually play it as you are supposed to.

RATING: 2
I understand how this game is rated so high, but I just can't get over how it really just isn't very fun.  It has some interesting mechanics, and I love how it is card-driven.  However, there are some key flaws: the debates are underwhelming, as it's really the advantage of maybe one extra card.  The issues generally are taken over initially and then rarely challenged.  The key states are really the only ones to fight over, and extra points are offered on the other ones.

Overall, the main issue is that the theme falls very flat for me.  I don't get the sense that I'm campaigning, as I can't keep track of whether I'm losing or winning at the polls.  Instead, I fight for area control in a few states and then hope that the final tally which is long and time-consuming results in a victory.  By contrast, Twilight Struggle has all the card-driven goodness plus the immediate feedback of where you stand by having a victory track.  Last but not least, I never feel the sense of accomplishment that I got something done, like finally getting a ship built in Le Havre or winning a scoring round in TS.  Simply put, this was disappointing, not fun, and I spent the two hours I played it wishing just about anything else was on the table.

RATING: 6.5
1960 is similar to Twilight Struggle, with some key differences. All cards have events. When an opponent plays your card for Campaign Points, the event only happens if you spend a momentum disk (obtained by leading in some key issues at the end of each round; and also from some cards). Any event that occurs sends that card out of the game. There's some randomness, with issues resolved by drawing cubes out of the bag. But you can affect that randomness by choosing to play cards for CP (which adds some cubes to the bag). You have to consider several areas on the board: regions, states, advertising, endorsements, issues. You have to prepare for a debate (choose which card to set aside each round) and finally for the elections. There are a lot of tough choices throughout; should play well once you actually know the rules!

After a couple of plays, I think I prefer Twilight Struggle. The constant back-and-forth scoring in TS adds a lot of tension to the game -- I miss that in 1960, where I feel like I'm just sort of floating along reacting to the other player, and then bam! the end is here and you see how you did. The bag works OK, but I like the Battle Bag in End of the Triumvirate better; somehow, the more limited use of the bag in EotT is easier to wrap my head around.

--- 
After a few more plays, I'm certain: I strongly prefer TS -- 1960 is sold!

RATING: 4
Have played this twice now and I think I've had my fill.  To be honest, I didn't enjoy playing 1960 much.  I feel a little guilty rating it on that criteria, but since I do it with other games, I should here, too.  There's a couple reasons I hesitate and even rated 1960 higher at first: first, I think it's one of those games where the first couples times are pretty much bound to be a bit of a struggle, where you spend the game trying to get the flow rather than focusing more on strategy and enjoyment; second, it's incredibly well-done and deserves to be recognized for being a great game of its type, even if it isn't -my- type of game.  That all said and told, though, it ends up being an somewhat unenjoyable affair for me, because I don't like the give-and-take this game affords.

Z-man did a fantastic presentation job with this game.  Everything looks just right; they managed to make the board interesting despite the fact that it's largely just a map of the U.S. (they even put a fake coffee mug ring on it to make it look like some sort of political war-room map).  The bits and cards are all high-quality, the little factoids on the cards are interesting.  A+ design here.

And the mechanics are really quite interesting and amazing.  The draw bag has that "mysterious game piece of power" feel to it, like the cube tower from Shogun or the Sauron die in Lord of the Rings.  You learn to respect and fear it.  The game -feels- thematically correct; there's never enough time to do everything, and it's a brutal tug-of-war for the states with the most electoral clout.  There's some mechanics that didn't click as well with me--like the momentum markers--but at least part of that is still my only vague grasp of how to play the game well. 

Despite all this, I just get the feeling it's not really a game I'll ever really enjoy.  It's essentially an incredibly rough war/area-control game where you fight claw and nail for every inch of progress.  I find games where everything you've done on your turn can be (and often is) flatly reversed by the opponent on their following turn (this was my problem with Coloretto Amazonas).  It's thematically appropriate here, but it's frustrating for me to spend two or three turns trading cubes on New York.  Maybe that would happen a bit less if I were more experienced--maybe I'd learn to focus attention on other things where my opponent wouldn't thwart me as much, I don't know--but as it was I felt like I barely kept the status quo for most of the game.  

Anyhow, short version: this game is amazingly designed and presented and you should give it at least a shot.  But, personally, it's not my type of game.

NIS
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[u]Key Words:[/u] Political - Card Driven - 2-player - Tit-for-Tat - 2h

[u]Summarized Opinion:[/u] Nice if somewhat frustrating game, good production, does a great job to reflect the theme. In the end, not satisfying.

[u]My Rating Explained:[/u] Ok game, will play sporadically if in the right mood.

[u]Verbose Assessment:[/u]

There's a few things that just don't sit right with me to give this game a higher rating.

The first is the impact of the cards on the gameplay. Basically, what cards you get and how you play them will win or lose you the game. While this is true of some games I rate a 10, in this game it annoys me. For one thing, every card has an event that's mostly negative for one of the players. If you're stuck with a hand full of cards that are good for your opponent, you will be in for a rough turn. It's possible to somewhat play around this effect using momentum, but only to a degree.

The second major thing I dislike about this game is the general flow of the game. The turns before the debates are essentially one big soup. It depends what both players choose to emphasize, bit it's entirely possible for an important state to swing wildly from one side to the other multiple times in a couple turns. In effect, your entire turn can theoretically be undone in a single turn of your opponent. Tit for tat, and to me, somewhat frustrating. Especially since in the end, that important state tends to be decided by some powerful event one of the players happened to draw.

Finally, the drawbag. It's an interesting, new-to-me mechanism, but I'm not sure I like it. In my first game, I lost by a hair because New York was recounted (ie, the draw bag went my opponent's way). The second, I lost by a hair because I failed to draw the majority out of the bag during a similar event. Both games could have gone either way.

In short, I'm sure a good player will win consistently against an inexperienced one, but the whole experience just feels frustrating and kinda pointless to me.

To end on a positive note: once the debates are over, the game is exciting and builds to a climax as the votes are tallied.

RATING: 4
Unlike its brother, 1960 plays oddly. There's a handful of important states (take Illinois to New Jersey and you're more or less set) and the binary nature of influence means there's a lot of back and forth. 

"Gathering Momentum" cards seem strangely game-defining. Get two in your early hand, and I imagine your opponent will sweat blood. I also miss the variable score times. We all know when the scoring will take place, who's got what and how that'll play out. No bluffing through this one. 

The choice of "momentum" is interesting, but removes some of the "how do I handle this" aspect that TS has. 


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This could have been an absolutely fantastic game but it is flawed in a few too many areas. It's production values are of the highest standard with excellent components, artwork etc, and the cards, which depict the major events of the respective presidential campaigns, provide interesting decisions and add to the theme brilliantly. On the downside however I feel that there's too much emphasis on the campaign actions where you increase state support by placing your cubes into various states. This feature of the gameplay is over-emphasised at the expense of the other options available to you, such as increasing media or issue support. The debate phase mechanics don't work as well as they should (placing cards of differing values against the campaign's three main issues in order to win the debate on them) and the benefits for being successful in this area (gaining a small amount of state support cubes) are too minimal to really feel as though they're having a significant impact on the game. There's much to admire here but the overall result is slightly underwhelming.

RATING: 8
2007-DEC-07, www.boardroomgames.com
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm a little concerned with the obvious slant of most of the cards. There are pro-Nixon, anti-Nixon, pro-Kennedy and anti-Kennedy cards. If I get my opponent's good cards or my own bad cards, then I don't have much to do in the way of burying it. I can put it in the debate, which usually means it helps them, or block it with two momentum tokens. Those aren't easy to come by, so getting dealt a whole hand of these in a turn will just be a huge help to your opponent. Given equal momentum, I can block one or two of these and they can execute the rest of the actions since it takes two of them to block and one to execute an event on an opponent's card. So, we can say that these card will even out, but then I can't conserve my momentum for these situations. For some reason the game makes me lose some of them if I don't use them. So you can explain these crappy card and momentum situations with a thematic explanation that the people running your campaign weren't doing there job.

If we start talking about theme, it seems wrong that the players are fighting over non-swing states. There's no way to lock anything down in this game so that it's not under threat anymore, so players might be fighting over Illinois and making multiple visits that don't really happen in elections. It makes a more interesting game, but doesn't work with the theme. So that's where some of the game goes wrong. It's got good potential, but it doesn't know if it wants to be a theme-game or a game-game and it mixes the two in ways that make you dislike the short-comings of each side.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game probably deserves a 9, but since I don't have that connection to the theme that is required in order to really appreciate the game, I can't give it more than a 7.

RATING: 5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I love TS, and so I really wanted to love this game too, since many of the mechanics are the same, and the theme appeals to me (although not nearly so much as TS).  But after a few plays, 1960 proves itself to be a much more one-dimensional game.  For those who griped about having to play their opponent's event cards in TS, yes, you can avoid triggering your opponent's events with momentum, and your opponent has to have momentum to spend even if you do play a card with one of his events, but there are many more events that are devastating to your position out of proportion to the value of the card, especially since many of them allow the play of influence anywhere, making damage control all but impossible, and hands filled with your opponents events devastating (unlike TS, where all but the very worst hands are actually quite manageable).  So the game naturally turns into a war over momentum, which means that the game turns into a tug-of-war over the issues.  The difficulty limiting the effect of your opponent's events is augmented by the fact that you effectively have to play all of the ones you get during the first five turns, since you must save cards with your own events, or neutral events, to play in the debates (unless you pick one issue to lose on, early - but that's a tricky and dangerous strategy).  And, lastly, what sticks in my craw the most - the debates don't have any effect on issue support!

This isn't a bad game, by any stretch, but I found it rather disappointing in comparison to TS.  The production values are beautiful, and it may be an even better 'crossover' game for non-gamers or Euro-gamers to get into CDGs.

Update: I really wanted to like this game much more than I do.  I started out rating it a 6, which after a few plays I felt was too harsh, and slowly raised my rating to 7.  But after playing a bit more, I have to say that there's really not much depth here, and while I like its good points less, I loathe its problems more.  I blow hot and cold on this one.

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 1
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 2
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 1
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 2

Overall - 1.5

(NH)
Letter Grade: B.  Does what it does well, but the same design system is already on full display in Twilight Struggle.  Still, the shorter gameplay makes it more accessible. [05-08-09]

After about a half-dozen plays (02-15-09): This has hit the table more times in a shorter period than many of the longer boardgames I own.  Chalk it up to: reasonable playing time; accessible theme and strategy; relatively forgiving system that isn't too hard to explain; thematic immersion.  The similarities between this and Twilight Struggle should not be denied, but I'm very happy to own both.  Is TS the "better" (read: thinkier, more heavyweight, more thematic) game?  Probably.  But I can actually squeeze a game of 1960 in on a weeknight.  Because most people just "get" what is important to win the presidency this game remains very accessible, and is a great introduction to the mechanics of card-driven games.

Some of this is due to the limitations imposed by the theme -- the scoring is necessarily at the end in 1960 while TS is more flexible and interesting. RATING: 7.5
A great card driven game which is essentially concerned with area control. A clever mechanic allows you to trigger an event on your opponent's card by paying a momentum marker. My first game was won by the Nixon player after he played a card which deducted -2 CP points from all Kenedy cards (on the last turn, of course). This gave him New York and the game. 1960 forces you to make difficult decisions every turn and is tension remains until the very end. It's no Twilight Struggle, but I did really enjoy the first play and will definitely be revisiting 1960 in the near future.

RATING: 6
SOLD (5/11/09) --- I think this game may be mechanically better than Twilight Struggle, but still not as enjoyable.  I really like two of the gameplay improvements in 1960 over Twilight Struggle.  First, the momentum markers for triggering events are a great addition.  Second, using the political capital bag rather than dice is a great change.  However, the lack of mid-game scoring in 1960 removes the tension and excitement that pervades Twilight Struggle.  In addition, the components of 1960 are also a good deal nicer than Twilight Struggle.  In the end though, 1960 just isn't nearly as tense as Twilight Struggle and feels a bit more leisurely since you know there won't be any scoring until the very end of the game.  I'm already looking forward to the third game in the series, hoping that combines the mid-game scoring of Twilight Struggle with the momentum markers and political capital bag mechanics from 1960... only time will tell.

Nice components, and very playable.  It helps to have been there and lived through that history, or at least to have been old enough to vaguely remember the politics of the day, though not essential.  A favorite 2 player. There is a lot of banter about which is better, 1960 or Twilight Struggle.  They are obviously very similar in overall game play, and they both represent their theme probably more accurately than any other games on the market, so I don't see any reason to set one above the other. Twilight Struggle gets the nod probably because one American Presidential election seems relatively insignificant next to the totality of the Cold War. Still, it is Top Notch!

RATING: 10
"In 1960: The Making of the President, you take on the role of one of these great protagonists vying for the right to lead his country into the heart of the Cold War. However, it is not just foreign policy that poses a challenge to American leadership; this is also an era of great social turmoil and progress. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
"Sometimes the history of a nation can be defined by the relationship between two individuals. The Election of 1960 is the story of two men, John F. Kennedy and Richard M. Nixon. One is the scion of a wealthy, politically powerful family from New England. The other is the son of a Quaker grocer in Whittier, California. While they belong to opposing political parties, they start out as friends. The complex development of that friendship, however, would shape a pivotal presidential election and cast a long shadow over American history for the remainder of the 20th century.

"In 1960: The Making of the President, you take on the role of one of these great protagonists vying for the right to lead his country into the heart of the Cold War. However, it is not just foreign policy that poses a challenge to American leadership; this is also an era of great social turmoil and progress. As the United States continues to build upon the promise of its founding, candidates must contend with the question of civil rights and balance their positions on social justice against the need for valuable Southern electoral votes. Of course, the ever-present issue of the economy also rears its ugly head, and both Nixon and Kennedy will compete to be the candidate with the voters' pocket books in mind.

"The contest is fought out on an electoral map of the United States as it stood in 1960—a map where Louisiana and Florida share the same number of electoral votes, as do California and Pennsylvania. Using a card-driven game system, all the major events which shaped the campaign are represented: Nixon’s lazy shave, President Eisenhower’s late endorsement, and the 'Catholic question' are all included as specific event cards. The famous televised debates and final election day push are each handled with their own subsystems. Candidates vie to capture each state’s electoral votes using campaign points in the four different regions of the country. At the same time, they must build momentum by dominating the issues of the day and attempt to gain control of the airwaves.

"As with any election campaign, the challenge is to adapt your game plan as the ground shifts out from under you. There are never enough resources or time to do everything, but you need to make the tough calls to propel yourself into the White House. This fast-playing strategy game for two players challenges you to run for the most powerful elective office in the world, at one of its most unique crossroads. Will you recreate history, or rewrite it? 1960: The Making of the President provides you the opportunity to do both."

RATING: 6
July 2016: Sold on Geek Market.

Initial impressions back in 2008: Great game. It's not Twilight Struggle, but the different theme and slight rule changes make this a great addition to my collection.

Update June 2016: My enjoyment of this game has steadily decreased the more I play Twilight Struggle. I like the election theme, but the gameplay and events don't excite me as much as the Cold War.

Twilight Struggle has NATO, the Cuban Missle Crisis, a Defcon track that can cause nuclear war, and an easier way to continuously keep score, which constantly reminds you how close you are to losing. 1960 on the other hand has a lot of boring events, a less harsh way of dealing with negative events, a debate round in the middle of the game that feels largely out of place, and no way to know how you're doing unless you can keep 50 numbers in your head or use an amazing app (available in the files section) that makes keeping score way easier, and allows a bit more campaign strategy.

Conclusion: A lighter version of Twilight Struggle, and a great game for politics fans. Game would be way better if they released a new deck and map after each US election, that way the events would be more up to date.

RATING: 9
Good balance, fun theme.

Compared to other card-driven games, 1960 event cards are generally more powerful and broad, allowing strong actions with few conditions or prerequisites.  To me this makes the game easier to play/teach, and a bit more fun.  You're not totally going along for the ride, but many of the event vs points decisions are easier when compared to twilight struggle or labyrinth or the COIN series.  Additionally since some spaces on the board are worth 5x-10x other spaces, beginners have a good idea where to focus, and less of a blank slate to work with.

RATING: 8.6
Grows on me with each play (I've played quite a bit online at Game Tables Online). There's a lot of tactical depth even despite the great importance of issues and the comparative insignificance of advertising.

However, because of the importance of the issues, there is a delicate balance when playing cards with powerful events. Players have to determine when it is worth giving up a turn of back and forth on the issues to play a particularly powerful event.

Creative card play to limit the negative effect of events activated by ones opponent is also an intriguing aspect of the game. The game gives so many options for mitigating their effects. One can:

1) Preempt events.
2) Use the candidate card to avoid playing another card.
3) Bury the card in Campaign Strategy.
4) Bluff with less damaging cards to run the opponent out of momentum.
5) Play a card at the end of the turn when it does little damage.
6) Influence the board to limit the effects of an event.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
1960 has a lot of good parts to it.  It has a great events system built on a shared deck which works very well.  It has several interesting subsystems, which model positioning on issues, the debates, and the whims of election day.  Unfortunately, 1960 also has an older brother named [gameid=12333].  And unfortunately, everything that I like about 1960 is kept and improved upon in Twilight Struggle.  In addition, TS improves upon 1960 in several key areas, namely tighter deck-management, lower luck factor, easier score tracking, and a much more interesting theme. The theme in 1960 is particularly troubling, especially how fast and loose states can change back and forth.  Either the political reality of 1960 is wildly different than it is today, or else it's really antithematic that some states would go red or blue in a close election.  I have a difficult time seeing New York going anything but blue, for instance.  It comes down to this: I would love 1960 if TS didn't exist, but I just can't justify bringing out this title when I can get a better experience elsewhere.

RATING: N/A
** Based on one play, I'm giving this a 9. I love the theme - I really felt I was fighting a rough election campaign, with all the swings, hostile media coverage and unexpected events. As someone who was heavily involved in youth politics, it captured the excitement of a real election campaign.

It is rough, combative and you have to be paying attention and thinking on your feet. A bad event can wreck long-term strategy, but I felt I could mitigate my several bad card draws by focusing on momentum/advertising/issues to swing the game. Not sure if that will continue with repeat plays.

Based on one play, I preferred it to Twilight Struggle, which felt a bit 'scripted'. 1960 doesn't play out like the real election - it is more flexible than that. I can see that, if you got really into TS, it would probably be a more controllable game.

RATING: 6
Having played a few rounds of Twilight Struggle, I was prepared to approach this game, and it probably helped a lot in learning it and moving straight in to it.  It definitely seems lighter, though it also moves a bit more quickly as a result.  The key mechanism of the game is in the cards, since they do a number of different things, and there's a nice metagame aspect to it at the beginning of each round, as you try to determine how to play your hand, and which cards to save for the debates and the election.  I didn't quite make it through my first game, but I look forward to the next one.

The second game saw some improvements, in that we think that we were playing the support draws from the bag incorrectly in our first game.  The different ways that the cards work in different circumstances, and the timing of playing the cards, is what gives the game its tension.  There are some key areas that deserve the most attention on the map, but it's important not to overlook the smaller states, also.

All told, it probably lacks some of the depth that players find in Twilight Struggle, but the fact that this game is usually over in about two hours, tops, whereas Twilight Struggle can last for up to five hours, makes it a nice game to have for when you have that itch, but don't want to devote that much time to a game.

2 plays: 2 players

RATING: N/A
In 1960: The Making of the President, you take on the role of one of these great protagonists vying to lead America through an era of turbulent change. The candidates must contend with all the great issues of the day, from the Cold War to civil rights to voters' pocket books. This is an election that will turn on positioning and momentum. The contest is fought on an electoral map of the United States as it stood in 1960. Using a card-driven game system, all the major events which shaped the campaign are represented: Nixon's lazy shave, President Eisenhower's late endorsement, and the 'Catholic question' are all specific event cards. The famous televised debates are also an important component of gameplay.
\13\\13\
As with a real election campaign, the challenge is to adapt your game plan as the ground shifts out from under you. There are never enough resources or time to do everything, but you need to make the tough calls to propel yourself into the White House. This fast-playing strategy game for two players challenges you to relive the most significant political contest of the Twentieth Century. Will you recreate history, or rewrite it? 1960: The Making of the President provides you the opportunity to do both.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Z-Man Games
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is an excellent card-driven game of campaign politics, based on the Nixon/Kennedy presidential election. Not only is it fun, it's also a damn good history lesson. I went into my first game of 1960 expecting to be a little bored by the theme. How wrong I was. Almost every event card that entered my hand made me say "No way...that actually happened?" I'd never fully understood why this election was considered such a pivotal event, aside from Kennedy going on to become a beloved American icon. (To be fair, I missed this particular party by about a decade and a half...I'm a Reagan era kid!) Needless to say, I thoroughly enjoyed the history lesson.

As for the actual nuts and bolts of the game, it's a solid adaptation of the Twilight Struggle system. The only feature I don't care for are the debates. I'm still not sure if they are meant to be used as a card dump or if they're actually something to focus your campaign strategy around. It doesn't seem like a great idea to burn strong cards in setting up for the it, since the rewards aren't overwhelmingly good. Yet if you don't prepare and just discard to it at random, then the whole phase can really burn you. That's a fairly minor complaint though, as the rest of the game is an entertaining and educational tug-of-war that keeps both players engaged right up until election night.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

RATING: 9
Very immersive.  Love that you are forced to constantly deal with the direct conflict of your opponent as you also attempt to implement a strategy for your candidate.  An amazing design. The best of all election style games.

I played a game with my daughter a few months ago which still makes me smile.  She was Kennedy, and I attempted to re-write history with Nixon.  Throughout the game I battled hard in the Northeast.  At one point I even gained New York and Massachusetts.  As I spent my time and resources in her backyard, she flew to the west coast a few times and poked me in California.  I was always able to rebuff her.

We battled on issues, neither of us really doing much for our campaigns, and we also hit hard on advertising.  She gained, then maintained the upper hand on that front.  The debates were a wash.  

On the last turn of the game, she flew to California again.  I thought, "Good.  Waste your time Sweetie, because I've got cards that are gonna win New York for me and maybe even Pennsylvania."

The cards we had been storing up for election day were revealed, support checks were made, and she kept New York, Massachusetts, and Pennsylvania while throwing me a bone in Ohio.  My eyes turned west and my heart sank as she stole California on support checks because of smart card play.

Not only did Kennedy win, but if Nixon would have been beaten in real life as soundly as I was, he never would have drew breath politically again.

I had a blast.  I thought of that game every day for some time.  That's what excellent games do. They tickle your brain and lighten your mood.

The tension 1960 creates makes a two hour game seem much shorter.  Replay value is high.  :)

RATING: 5
I have some concerns.  'Issues' seem an oddly abstracted concept (what exactly is playing Ops into Issues representing?) while simultaneously of commanding importance, almost always more than that of campaigning or media buying.  Media buying, a huge part of modern campaigns, is completely neglected and of tertiary importance.  There is no card management in the game, and no real chance for strategic movement.  In short, I feel as if the designers, in attempting to make a 'TS-lite', have instead made the game a bit facile instead.  Still, I do like the topic, though it's a bit too long to be truly classified as a 'filler' game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
After two plays of this game, I am in love with it.  I only wish I could read my wife a little better.  I know she doesn't particularly like the length of the game.  Part of it her fault - she has a little AP syndrome.  Her first move of the game took 10 minutes - I timed it!  I hope as she becomes more familiar it will streamline.  If we could play in 60-90 minutes I am sure it would hit the table more often.

Other than that, there is nothing I don't like about this game.  It is so realistic.  I haven't played another game where the mechanics and the theme meld so well together.  It is so much like real life it is scary.  There are about a million things you want to do but are limited by your time and money and have to do what is best at the time.  However, there are events taking place all around you that are out of your control.  As Kennedy, you want to campaign in the West but Congress calls an emergency session and you must do your job - fly back to Maryland and suffer a penalty to movement.  Or as Nixon you bang your knee on your car door and are in the hospital unable to campaign at all.

There have been some people who don't particularly like the debate section.  I think it is an interesting part of the game.  Winning the last issue allows you to put four state support anywhere on the board.  Two turns from election day, this could put a serious hamper on your opponent's final campaign strategy.  Trying to figure out how to play your cards without getting burned by opponent's events and placing the right cards into the debates (right issue, right candidate, right CP value) makes for an interesting challenge.  I know my wife has been frustrated by her lack of ability to play the debates well and me being able to put a lot of state support onto the board.

RATING: 6
This game devolves in the same way that real-life US elections do - inevitably both players are fighting over 6 or so huge states and completely ignoring the lower-population territories. It does a good job of realism, but I'm not sure it makes a particularly interesting game. The debate mechanic sets this apart from other CDGs, but that alone doesn't do enough for me to prefer playing this game to others in the genre.

RATING: 8
I love the theme of American elections. Great card driven placement game. You learn a lot about the time period. Easy to learn rules. Thanks to this game i know where all states are :P

Only one element feels a little bit confusing : the debates. But every thing else feels good and within the theme of the boardgame. Both factions seem to be balanced.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
10/30/07 - Well, after 1 play, I have a few comments.  First of all, I felt like the theme was sort of hollow.  The initial draw for me to the game was the unusual subject matter, so this was sort of a let down.  I say the theme is hollow because it expected me to remember the election a bit better then I do (which is not at all).  Perhaps it's just that US presidential elections are not as grand a topic as some of this game's competition.  I'm not sure yet.

The game also seemed to lack some of the tension I would have thought it should have.  Though this can easily be chalked up to inexperience in terms of me not understanding all of the ramifications of my actions, I felt that it was more because I had no idea who was winning/losing because I was not spending any time continually counting and recounting electoral votes to see who was in the lead.  So instead of the give and take of tough decision making, I just always chose whatever I thought increased my relative position by the largest margin.  Maybe more experience with the game would fix this, but at this time it's too soon to tell.  Just about any game deserves a second try, and I intend on giving one to 1960, but at this time I'm in no hurry to do so.
----
Pretty much what appears to be the USA's response to Die Macher.  We'll see if this completely non-existent comparison holds up in court.

RATING: 7.5
I played this once as Nixon. I didn't quite have a grasp of the debate mechanics, so I went in at a disadvantage, but won 2 of 3, so it wasn't so bad. I didn't have any Warfare (or whatever it's called) cards in my hand for the debate, but I was strong in the other two issues. The rest of the game was area control and hand management, both of which seemed mechanically pretty intuitive. 

The second game it went much more smoothly.  The game is quite elegant.  Rating raised.
